  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_657bf782-c173-4c7d-b39e-d732e56116fa'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer11176616432088958025">In the house of Helios, god of the sun and mightiest of the Titans, a daughter is born.   <span id="freeText11176616432088958025" style="display:none">In the house of Helios, god of the sun and mightiest of the Titans, a daughter is born. But Circe is a strange child—not powerful, like her father, nor viciously alluring like her mother. Turning to the world of mortals for companionship, she discovers that she does possess power—the power of witchcraft, which can transform rivals into monsters and menace the gods themselves.<br /><br />Threatened, Zeus banishes her to a deserted island, where she hones her occult craft, tames wild beasts and crosses paths with many of the most famous figures in all of mythology, including the Minotaur, Daedalus and his doomed son Icarus, the murderous Medea, and, of course, wily Odysseus.<br /><br />But there is danger, too, for a woman who stands alone, and Circe unwittingly draws the wrath of both men and gods, ultimately finding herself pitted against one of the most terrifying and vengeful of the Olympians. To protect what she loves most, Circe must summon all her strength and choose, once and for all, whether she belongs with the gods she is born from, or the mortals she has come to love.</span>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1336931-are-there-rape-scenes-in-this-book-if-so">Are there rape scenes in this book? If so, are they graphic? Please help.</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1336931'><a id="like_count_community_question_1336931" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1336931?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">45 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F35959740-circe">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/1336931-are-there-rape-scenes-in-this-book-if-so">2 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/5000148-judy-lindow"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1383546760i/5000148._UX100_CR0,19,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/5000148-judy-lindow">Judy Lindow</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    (spoiler) The rape, IMO, is the linchpin for Circe's defining moment That she is known for turning men into pigs and not for being raped, scolded, out<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    (spoiler) The rape, IMO, is the linchpin for Circe's defining moment That she is known for turning men into pigs and not for being raped, scolded, outcast, ignored; she will protect herself, get even, and make independent decisions and actions rather than be made a victim who waits for a hero. Not a lot of time was spent on describing what happened, it wasn't explicit, but it was realistic and probable. Perhaps being told in the 1st person made it more realistic - the circumstance - her being alone, independent, and having means, made her a target -- that is was what made it realistic to me. Her reaction, actions, understanding of it and precautions after that were retributive for me. I also thought the way the author wrote it - in the same way she talked about Circe's love life - all seemed to match the voice and character of the woman ... it sort of took me a paragraph further on to fully understand what had just happened. Yes, I'm a little slow, but I also think it's part of the author's capacity to share important or traumatic experiences without being exploitive. I think one reason I'd never enjoyed myths before was they were like fairytales. This book made everything more plausible by providing the back story and what motivated the characters. <a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>
</div>

<div id="review_2253165934" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2253165934" />
    <a title="Melanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3030788-melanie"><img alt="Melanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577914102p2/3030788.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2253165934?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melanie" href="/user/show/3030788-melanie">Melanie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=retellings">retellings</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=arc">arc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=buddy-reads">buddy-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=magic">magic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=signed">signed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=read-in-2018">read-in-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=witches">witches</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2253165934" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9144184843635627259"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524348099i/25413681._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><i>ARC provided by the publisher via Netgalley in exchange for an honest review.</i><br/><br/>✨ Signed and personalized copies are available through <a href='https://www.mainpointbooks.com/madeline-miller' rel='nofollow noopener'>Main Point Books</a>! (They can ship anywhere in the US, anywhere in the UK, and also to some other international locations)<br/><br/><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“When I was born, the name for what I was did not exist.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br/>This is the pièce de résistance I’ve been searching for my entire life. Not only did I fall in love with this story, I predict that this will be the best book I’ll read all year. </span>
  <span id="freeText9144184843635627259" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524348099i/25413681._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>ARC provided by the publisher via Netgalley in exchange for an honest review.</i><br><br>✨ Signed and personalized copies are available through <a href="https://www.mainpointbooks.com/madeline-miller" rel="nofollow noopener">Main Point Books</a>! (They can ship anywhere in the US, anywhere in the UK, and also to some other international locations)<br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“When I was born, the name for what I was did not exist.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br>This is the pièce de résistance I’ve been searching for my entire life. Not only did I fall in love with this story, I predict that this will be the best book I’ll read all year. This book is about healing and doing what it takes to come into your own. This book is about love; the love between lovers, the love of a mother, and the love you must find in yourself. This book proves why family of choice will always be greater than family of origin. This book is about magic, and how we can find it in ourselves if we look hard enough. This is a book about becoming the witch you’ve always buried deep inside you. <br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“They do not care if you are good. They barely care if you are wicked. The only thing that makes them listen is power.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br>Okay, maybe I should start this review off with a somewhat personal story. I was very privileged to go a very good high school where I was able to study <i>The Iliad</i> and <i>The Odyssey</i> for a class my freshman year. And fourteen-year-old Melanie fell in love. To say I was obsessed was an understatement, and more and more my heart was filled with love for Odysseus, Athena, and a certain love affair with the witch-goddess Circe. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1522296139i/25288839._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>(Beautiful art by <a href="https://kevinnichols.deviantart.com/art/Circe-2-363511884" rel="nofollow noopener">Kevin Nichols</a>) <br><br>Even upon finishing that class, I still couldn’t get enough of Homer’s words. And to this day, <i>The Iliad</i> and <i>The Odyssey</i> are the only books that I collect many editions of. All my loved ones and family correlate these epic poems with me, and always bring me new editions from their travels, and give me gifts for special events and holidays the same way they do with <i>Harry Potter</i>. One of the most prized possession I own is an edition of <i>The Odyssey</i> that was given to me by someone who meant a lot to me, at a very important time in my life. And these two tomes will always be a big part of my identity, and I will always recognize that they not only shaped me as a reader, but they shaped me as a human being, too. <br><br>So, when I found out that that Greek mythology retelling queen, Madeline Miller, was writing a book centered around Circe, I knew it was going to end up being one of my favorite books of all time. And it ended up being everything I wanted and more. I hate to throw around the word masterpiece, but if I had to pick a book to give that title to, I’d pick <i>Circe</i>. <br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“Odysseus, son of Laertes, the great traveler, prince of wiles and tricks and a thousand ways. He showed me his scars, and in return he let me pretend that I had none.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br>And even though Odysseus plays a huge role in this story, this book is Circe’s and Circe’s alone. We get to see her growing up in Oceanus, with her Titan sun god father Helios, and loveless nymph mother Perse, and her three more ambitious siblings, Aeëtes, Pasiphaë, and Perses. We get to see her living her life of solitude, exiled on the island of Aiaia. We also get to see her make a few very important trips, that are very monumental in Greek mythos. But we get to see all of Circe, the broken parts, the healing parts, and the complete parts. We get to see her love, her loss, her discovery, her resolve, and her determination. We get to see her question what it means to be immortal, what it means to be a nymph in a world ruled by gods, and what it means to just live. Her journey is unlike anything I’ve ever read before, and probably unlike anything I will ever read again. I have no combination of words to express how much her life and her story means to me. But I promise, I’m not the same person I was before reading this book.<br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“…All my life had been murk and depths, but I was not a part of that dark water. I was a creature within it.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br>This is ultimately a story about how different the tales will always be told for a man. And how the ballads will always be sung for heroes, not heroines, even if a woman was truly behind all the success the man greedily reaped. <b>How the light will always fall to vilify the woman and showcase her as a witch that needs to be tamed, a sorceress that needs to be subdued, or an enchantress that needs to be defeated.</b> Women, no matter how much agency they carve out in any male dominated world, will always be a means to an end to further the achievements of man. Always. And <i>Circe</i> displays that at the forefront of this story. <br><br>Circe is most well known for turning Odysseus’s men into pigs when they come to her island in <i>The Odyssey</i>, but Madeline Miller does such a wonderful job weaving all this Greek mythology into a fully fleshed out, brand-new tale. She has created something so unique, yet so breathtakingly good, I think so many readers will find it impossible to put this new-spin of a story down. I was completely captivated and enthralled from the very first line to the very last line. This book just feels so authentic, I felt like I was in the ocean, on the island, and traveling right beside Circe throughout. And I never wanted to leave her side. <br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“It was their favorite bitter joke: those who fight against prophecy only draw it more tightly around their throats.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br>Overall, I understand that this is a book that is very targeted to me and my likes. Not only is this a character driven story, with a main protagonist being a character I’ve been in love with for over a decade, but the writing was lyrical perfection. I’m such a quote reader, and I swear I would have highlighted this entire book. This book is also so beautifully feminist that it makes me weep just thinking about the things Circe had to endure. And it showcases the unconditional love of found families, yet also between a mother and her child, while simultaneously abolishing the notion that blood is worth more than anything else in any world. This book heavily emphasizes that you will never be the mistakes that your parents have committed. The entire story is a love letter to love itself and reveals all the things we are willing to do in the name of it. And most importantly, this is a book about how we are truly only ever in charge of our own stories, even though our actions may change the fate for others around us. Please, pick this masterpiece up, and I hope it changes your life, too. <br><br>Thank you, Madeline Miller, I will carry your Circe in my heart for the rest of my life. <br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“That is one thing gods and mortals share: when we are young, we think ourselves the first to have each feeling in the world.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br><b>Trigger/Content Warnings:</b> Violence, gore, murder, torture, physical abuse, child abuse, thoughts of suicide, brief scene with cutting, graphic childbirth scenes, mention of bestiality, mention of incest, animal sacrifice, death of a sibling, death of a child, death of a loved one, death of an animal, rape, adultery, and war themes. <br><br><a href="https://meltotheany.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.twitter.com/meltotheany" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="http://meltotheany.tumblr.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Tumblr</a> | <a href="https://www.instagram.com/meltotheany/" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://www.youtube.com/channel/UC4RbHEg_fH6CewJrh_0xJ9g" rel="nofollow noopener">Youtube</a> | <a href="http://www.twitch.tv/meltotheany" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitch</a> <br><br><i>The quotes above were taken from an ARC and are subject to change upon publication.</i><br><br>Buddy read with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/52545435-elise-thebookishactress" rel="nofollow noopener">Elise</a> (My French Spider Queen)! ❤</span>
  <a data-text-id="9144184843635627259" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1953672073" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1953672073" />
    <a title="Elle (ellexamines)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52545435-elle-ellexamines"><img alt="Elle (ellexamines)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582250895p2/52545435.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1953672073?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elle (ellexamines)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elle (ellexamines)" href="/user/show/52545435-elle-ellexamines">Elle (ellexamines)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=z-read2018">z-read2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=x-arcs">x-arcs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=z-faves2018">z-faves2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=genre-experiments">genre-experiments</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=sff-high-fantasy">sff-high-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=favorite-characters">favorite-characters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=favorite-squads">favorite-squads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=author-love">author-love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1953672073" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2978073917521955325"><blockquote>
  <i>You threw me to the crows, but it turns out I prefer them to you.</i>
</blockquote><br/>My words are not as good as the ones in this book. <i>Circe</i> is a book about... finding yourself. But god, <b>
  <u>it stands out so far from just that.</u>
</b> <br/><br/>Okay, to get started, I’m just going to say it: <b>
  <u>Madeline Miller is one of the best writers of our time.</u>
</b> She has such a way with words that it is absolutely impossible not to be engaged in her storytelling. <br/><br/>The thing that brings this whole novel together is <b>
  <u>Circe’s character.</u>
</b> She</span>
  <span id="freeText2978073917521955325" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>You threw me to the crows, but it turns out I prefer them to you.</i>
</blockquote><br />My words are not as good as the ones in this book. <i>Circe</i> is a book about... finding yourself. But god, <b>
  <u>it stands out so far from just that.</u>
</b> <br /><br />Okay, to get started, I’m just going to say it: <b>
  <u>Madeline Miller is one of the best writers of our time.</u>
</b> She has such a way with words that it is absolutely impossible not to be engaged in her storytelling. <br /><br />The thing that brings this whole novel together is <b>
  <u>Circe’s character.</u>
</b> She is a woman who has done awful, evil things, and yet remains unfailingly human. She is lonely, and harsh, and hiding herself in sarcasm much of the time. And there is not a moment in this novel in which I didn’t adore her. Madeline Miller does such an amazing job developing this character, weaving her thoughts into the narrative without manipulating you into feeling a certain way, keeping the narrative wide yet keeping it focused around Circe. Throughout this novel I developed such a deep level of admiration for both this author and this character, <b>
  <u>this character I’m sure will stay with me forever.</u>
</b><br /><br />This novel is so interesting because at its core, it is an exploration of the voice of women in Greek mythology. Circe is a character we see nothing of in the narrative of Greek mythology, a character with seemingly evil intentions and little motivation – and all this despite showing up in several different stories. There’s something supremely excellent about seeing a character like this who is essentially a plot device be given a story. I know I have a tendency to repeat the term “narrative agency” but it beats repeating—<b>
  <u>I absolutely love giving characters who have been given no agency the agency they deserve.</u>
</b><br /><br />I mean, everything about this book was just brilliant. I loved the myth interpretation: Penelope and Odysseus are both written perfectly, and seeing Jason basically get called an asshole while Medea stood on being young and morally grey and in love was so fantastic. And the exploration of gods vs. mortals is just brilliant: <blockquote>
  <i>You cannot know how frightened gods are of pain. There is nothing more foreign to them, and so nothing they ache more deeply to see.</i>
</blockquote><br />I loved the relationships — just as a special note, the relationship between Circe and Telegonus made me want to cry. I basically loved everything. <br /><br />I mean, I think you guys have gotten pretty easily why I liked this so much — <b>
  <u>a morally-grey-character-driven retelling revolving around agency is basically my entire what-I-like bio.</u>
</b> This did all the things I like and I want to reread it daily and hourly. I very well might. <br /><br />[I also want you all to know this book gave rise to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user_status/show/168195356" rel="nofollow noopener">my favorite update meme I have ever posted</a> so thanks for that!!]<br /><br /><a href="https://thebookishactress.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/52545435" rel="nofollow noopener">Goodreads</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/jespergayhey" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="http://www.youtube.com/channel/UCVIY_GxZIuclzsIw9CLSe3g" rel="nofollow noopener">Youtube</a><br />buddyread with my favorite <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2253165934?book_show_action=false" rel="nofollow noopener">Melanie</a> 💜</span>
  <a data-text-id="2978073917521955325" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1953672073">

<div id="review_2252366313" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2252366313" />
    <a title="Emily May" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may"><img alt="Emily May" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528390751p2/4622890.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2252366313?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily May" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may">Emily May</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=botm">botm</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=arc">arc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2252366313" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2890194990852067226"><blockquote>
  <i>“Witches are not so delicate,” I said.</i>
</blockquote><br/><b>I absolutely loved this</b>. If you enjoy Greek mythology, complex heroines, and a generous serving of adventure, bloodshed, betrayal, magic, and monsters - both literal and figurative - then hell, READ THIS BOOK.<br/><br/>To be honest, I wasn't a huge fan of Miller's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11250317.The_Song_of_Achilles' title='The Song of Achilles by Madeline Miller' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Song of Achilles</a> when I read it a few years back. I'm not sure if that's because my tastes were different back then, or if it was just because the plot had more of a romantic focus than <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35959740.Circe' title='Circe by Madeline Miller' rel='nofollow noopener'>Circe</a>. But, w</span>
  <span id="freeText2890194990852067226" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“Witches are not so delicate,” I said.</i>
</blockquote><br /><b>I absolutely loved this</b>. If you enjoy Greek mythology, complex heroines, and a generous serving of adventure, bloodshed, betrayal, magic, and monsters - both literal and figurative - then hell, READ THIS BOOK.<br /><br />To be honest, I wasn't a huge fan of Miller's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11250317.The_Song_of_Achilles" title="The Song of Achilles by Madeline Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">The Song of Achilles</a> when I read it a few years back. I'm not sure if that's because my tastes were different back then, or if it was just because the plot had more of a romantic focus than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35959740.Circe" title="Circe by Madeline Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">Circe</a>. But, whatever the reason, I had no such problem with this book. <b>I was absolutely captivated from start to finish.</b><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35959740.Circe" title="Circe by Madeline Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">Circe</a> is part beautifully-written literary fantasy and part divine Greek soap opera. This strange combination makes for a book that is extremely quotable, rich in description and detail, and also a pageturner. It moves seamlessly between the broader scope of the world and its many gods and monsters, to the more narrow focus of the nymph-turned-witch, Circe, and her daily life before and after she is exiled to the island Aeaea.<br /><br />Circe becomes a powerful witch, but the strength of her story is in all her relatable flaws and weaknesses. We follow her as a naive lesser nymph, longing to be accepted and loved. We stay with her as she believes the lies of others and, later, becomes hardened against such deceivers. Her compassion constantly battles with her rage. Understandably.<br /><br />There is some grim satisfaction to be gained as this woman who has been bullied, belittled and trod on her entire life slowly claws out some vengeance for herself. The pain she endures along the way means that her successes are bittersweet. <b>In the end, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35959740.Circe" title="Circe by Madeline Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">Circe</a> might be full of fantasy, backstabbing and murder, but it is first and foremost the story of one woman's life - through pain, love, desire, heartache and motherhood.</b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>I did not go easy to motherhood. I faced it as soldiers face their enemies, girded and braced, sword up against the coming blows. Yet all my preparations were not enough.</i>
</blockquote><br />Other Greek myths play out in the background - that of the Minotaur, and of Icarus, as well as many others - but it is Circe's personal tale that hits the hardest. I just hope we don't have to wait another seven years for Miller to write another novel like this.<br /><br />TW: Rape; graphic violence.<br /><br /><a href="http://thebookgeek.org" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.facebook.com/thebookgeekreviews" rel="nofollow noopener">Facebook</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/thebookgeek01" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="http://instagram.com/emleemay" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://www.youtube.com/c/EmilyMayTheBookGeek" rel="nofollow noopener">Youtube</a></span>

<div id="review_2307894765" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2307894765" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2307894765?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781408890080" href="/book/show/32993458-circe">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=ancient-greece">ancient-greece</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=reviewed-for-fantasy-book-review">reviewed-for-fantasy-book-review</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=5-star-reads">5-star-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2307894765" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4303747203859140841">This is a beautiful book; it is flawless and intelligent. I do not have a single criticism for this fantastic piece of writing. <b>I loved it!</b><br/><br/><i>Circe</i> chronicles the life of a lesser god. She is the daughter of the mighty God Helios, the living embodiment of the sun. She is born without any particular talents or powers. She exists in the shadows of her more developed brothers and sisters. She does not shine in such spectacular company. <br/><br/>However, gifts come in many different forms and those with hidden </span>
  <span id="freeText4303747203859140841" style="display:none">This is a beautiful book; it is flawless and intelligent. I do not have a single criticism for this fantastic piece of writing. <b>I loved it!</b><br /><br /><i>Circe</i> chronicles the life of a lesser god. She is the daughter of the mighty God Helios, the living embodiment of the sun. She is born without any particular talents or powers. She exists in the shadows of her more developed brothers and sisters. She does not shine in such spectacular company. <br /><br />However, gifts come in many different forms and those with hidden talents are overlooked and devalued. More often than not quiet people are forgotten about and there worth ill-considered in all walks of existence. Circe’s family never saw what she could become. Power is important, though sometimes having none teaches one a greater lesson: nothing is worth having unless it has been earned. As such Circe wills herself into power as she discovers her affinity for witchcraft, especially the art of transformation.<br /><br />Her family banish her from their company for her use of such a lowly art, and in doing so they set her free. She finds herself in her exile. On her island home she finds a paradise not a prison. She becomes one with nature and finds company with lions and wolves. Centuries pass, ages pass, and eventually some rather important characters come her way. She meets Hermes and Athena, Icarus and his farther Daedalus, and Odysseus, a man who changes her life and causes her to make a very powerful decision that leads this book into such an excellent conclusion.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>“But in a solitary life, there are rare moments when another soul dips near yours, as stars once a year brush the earth. Such a constellation was he to me.” </i>
</b><br /><br /><i>Circe</i> offers a huge story, a story that spans generations and includes many Greek heroes and gods. Such is the nature of godhood, of immortality. When life goes on forever many notable people cross one’s path. And despite the huge number of famous characters here, none of it felt forced: it all slotted perfectly into Circe’s life. There are so many myths that intertwine with Circe, like the story of the Minotaur and the fall of Icarus, though despite the famous nature of many of them they don’t for a second overshadow her. <br /><br />She met Prometheus when she was young and decided that her life would not be the same as the other gods: she was going to be her own woman. And this is a book about her finding the most ultimate form of freedom. I could not recommend it more highly. I really liked <i>The Song of Achilles</i> though this surpassed it in every way. I really hope to see more from this author in the future. <br /><br /><b> Five fantastic stars </b><br /><br />___________________________________<br /><br />You can connect with me on social media via <a href="https://linktr.ee/sean.ultra_vegan1" rel="nofollow noopener">My Linktree</a>.<br />__________________________________</span>
  <a data-text-id="4303747203859140841" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2254005614" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6545238501766250275">Do you ever just get mad because you’re spending your life paying rent and wishing you could take a break from the linear flow of time when you could have been a goddess living in an enchanted island that is only seen every ten years and is unreachable by men? You live with ancient queens of myth and you're so much a part of each other it was like a second soul inside your skin. You sing hymns, burn incenses, and make fragrant oils. You call birds to sing at your windows, fall asleep in patches </span>
  <span id="freeText6545238501766250275" style="display:none">Do you ever just get mad because you’re spending your life paying rent and wishing you could take a break from the linear flow of time when you could have been a goddess living in an enchanted island that is only seen every ten years and is unreachable by men? You live with ancient queens of myth and you're so much a part of each other it was like a second soul inside your skin. You sing hymns, burn incenses, and make fragrant oils. You call birds to sing at your windows, fall asleep in patches of magical herbs with hair dripping all around you like a shawl and sit by the hearth with a lioness, cheeks glowing with the flames' light. Your skin is all luster and gleam and you're so far removed from the world's sorrows and so at peace and healthy. You live into 300 years, and in the space where legends and fairytales are gathering up words, your names are amongst them.<br /><br />Yeah, same.<br /><br /><blockquote>☆ <a href="https://ko-fi.com/givemeabrekk" rel="nofollow noopener">ko-fi</a> ★ <a href="https://bookswithchaima.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">blog</a> ☆ <a href="https://twitter.com/proyearner" rel="nofollow noopener">twitter</a> ★ <a href="https://wylansvaneck.tumblr.com" rel="nofollow noopener">tumblr</a> ☆</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="6545238501766250275" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2363862946" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2363862946" />
    <a title="Bibi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33411728-bibi"><img alt="Bibi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602128191p2/33411728.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2363862946?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bibi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bibi" href="/user/show/33411728-bibi">Bibi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/37552456-circe">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2363862946" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17933149472349144470"><blockquote><b>*Spoilers*<br/><br/>How I wish Miller's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35959740.Circe' title='Circe by Madeline Miller' rel='nofollow noopener'>Circe</a> was a reimagining as opposed to a retelling</b> and I say this because there's little else about Greek mythology that isn't readily available online or at the library. <br/><br/>A reinterpretation, on the other hand, gives an author creative license to weave a uniquely extravagant and fantastical story ( <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40669906.Now_I_Rise__The_Conqueror_s_Saga__2_' title='Now I Rise (The Conqueror's Saga #2) by Kiersten White' rel='nofollow noopener'>Now I Rise</a> did it perfectly) and perhaps one in which a lowly nymph attains great powers, transforms into a formidable sorceress who then proceeds to defy and defeat gods. </blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText17933149472349144470" style="display:none"><blockquote><b>*Spoilers*<br /><br />How I wish Miller's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35959740.Circe" title="Circe by Madeline Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">Circe</a> was a reimagining as opposed to a retelling</b> and I say this because there's little else about Greek mythology that isn't readily available online or at the library. <br /><br />A reinterpretation, on the other hand, gives an author creative license to weave a uniquely extravagant and fantastical story ( <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40669906.Now_I_Rise__The_Conqueror_s_Saga__2_" title="Now I Rise (The Conqueror's Saga #2) by Kiersten White" rel="nofollow noopener">Now I Rise</a> did it perfectly) and perhaps one in which a lowly nymph attains great powers, transforms into a formidable sorceress who then proceeds to defy and defeat gods. <br /><br />But, I digress.<br /><br />If Miller's ultimate goal is to introduce Greek mythology to a new generation of readers, then, I think she succeeded. <b>However, that's ALL she achieved.</b><br /><br />This story about an inferior but immortal nymph called, Circe, who is a progeny of not one but TWO Titans -Helios and Oceanus- is decidedly underwhelming, trite, and overwrought with both too many characters yet very little story progression. Presumedly, the author had a checklist of events (and characters) that simply had to make an appearance in the story, even if the tangent was superfluous and unrelated:<br /><br />Prometheus, and the banishment. Check<br />Scylla, the six-headed monster. Check<br />Pasiphae, Daedalus, the Bull of Poseidon, and the horror that was Minotaur. Check<br />Let's not forget, Odyssey.<br />And Hermes<br />And Athena<br />And many others who <i>(please listen closely)</i> <u>WERE NOT REQUIRED TO MOVE THIS STORY FORWARD.</u> Think I'm making this up? <br /><br /><b>Well, let's see what the story's about shall we?</b><br /><br />1. Circe is so dull and uninteresting that<br />2. Pretty much everyone ignores her; that is, until... <br />3. She uses her magic to turn Scylla into the six-headed monster. <br />4. Consequently, she's exiled to an island<br />5. Where she at times turned unsavoury sailors into pigs<br />6. Eventually leaving the island only after having lived there for centuries.<br />7. The end.<br /><br /><b>All in all, I think if you're new to mythology then this is for you; but even then I'd recommend reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33970092.Greek_Mythology_A_Captivating_Guide_to_the_Ancient_Gods__Goddesses__Heroes__and_Monsters__Norse_Mythology___Egyptian_Mythology___Greek_Mythology_Book_3_" title="Greek Mythology A Captivating Guide to the Ancient Gods, Goddesses, Heroes, and Monsters (Norse Mythology - Egyptian Mythology - Greek Mythology Book 3) by Matt Clayton" rel="nofollow noopener">Greek Mythology: A Captivating Guide to the Ancient Gods, Goddesses, Heroes, and Monsters</a> instead.</b></blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="17933149472349144470" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2617273870" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2617273870" />
    <a title=" Teodora " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/69603149-teodora"><img alt=" Teodora " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582144281p2/69603149.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2617273870?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title=" Teodora " class="user" itemprop="url" name=" Teodora " href="/user/show/69603149-teodora"> Teodora </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781408890042" href="/book/show/41045102-circe">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69603149-teodora?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69603149-teodora?shelf=on-app">on-app</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69603149-teodora?shelf=retellings">retellings</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69603149-teodora?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69603149-teodora?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69603149-teodora?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2617273870" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1555526946058307871">4.45/5 ⭐<br/><br/><b>Full review on my Blog</b>: <a href='https://thedacianshewolf.home.blog/2019/03/25/circe-by-madeline-miller/' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Dacian She-Wolf</a> 🐺<br/><br/><b>”Witches are not so delicate”.</b><br/>Witches are raw power. And raw power through its definition is not delicate.<br/><br/>Being honest right now, I have never been a huge fan of Greek mythology. I used to be obsessed with Egyptian mythology and fascinated by the Celtic one. But Greek mythology seemed all Greek to me (pun intended!). Not because it was unattractive, but just because I never understood it properly. I felt like it was a never-ending tale, too el</span>
  <span id="freeText1555526946058307871" style="display:none">4.45/5 ⭐<br /><br /><b>Full review on my Blog</b>: <a href="https://thedacianshewolf.home.blog/2019/03/25/circe-by-madeline-miller/" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dacian She-Wolf</a> 🐺<br /><br /><b>”Witches are not so delicate”.</b><br />Witches are raw power. And raw power through its definition is not delicate.<br /><br />Being honest right now, I have never been a huge fan of Greek mythology. I used to be obsessed with Egyptian mythology and fascinated by the Celtic one. But Greek mythology seemed all Greek to me (pun intended!). Not because it was unattractive, but just because I never understood it properly. I felt like it was a never-ending tale, too elaborate for my capacity of understanding. But this book written by Madeline Miller, <b>Circe</b>, helped the eyes of my mind flush open with understanding and for this I am thankful. <br /><br />Circe is introduced to us as a nobody. She is neither a full Goddess, in the true meaning of the concept, nor a mudblooded mortal. She is a Goddess, the daughter of the mighty Sun, the Titan Helios, and the beautiful nymph Perse, but the mundanity of her voice and the inutility of her presence made her a lesser Goddess in other Gods' point of view, even lesser than universally considered. She is an outcast even in her own family and as hard as she wants to change this, she’s bound by unseen powers not to (Fates, duh!).<br /><br />The book expands some universal and social ideas: the road of an outcast hero, searching for love in a loveless life, feminist actions abnormal for the collective mentality of the time, vengeance and forgiveness. So many more.<br /><br />Starting with the road of an outcast hero, all is there to say is if you are not outcasted, then you don’t suffer and if you don’t suffer you have no drive to make something worth out of your life. In this case, an infinite, immortal life. Circe’s exile on the isle of Aiaia makes her realise the infinite power that she possesses within her and everywhere around her. It all starts with a plant. It all ends with the same plant.<br /><br /><i>Pharmaka</i> is associated in the book with witchcraft. The power of the herbs to heal is magical. In my native language, Romanian, there is a word similar to the word pharmaka and its meaning from the book. We say <i>farmece</i>, witchery spells. When someone does farmece to you, that person casts an unseen and unfelt spell over you (both metaphorically and practically speaking). Circe’s power is to tame the so-feared pharmaka and use it without being afraid. <br /><br />Circe is a powerful individual, but she gains this power from her wisdom, the wisdom of time: <b>””You are wise”, he said. ”If it is so”, I said, ”it is only because I have been a fool enough for a hundred lifetimes””</b>. She is a free spirit and she has her father’s roots of pride. She is her own master and she doesn't agree with the ancient etiquettes, sung by bards and stuck for eternity in people’s minds: <b>„Humbling women seems to me a chief pastime of poets. As if there can be no story unless we crawl and weep”</b>. This shows the power of freedom she raised in herself.<br /><br />Circe is powerful, yes. Circe is unique. Circe is herself. She gives us a valuable lesson on how to live free and how to live sufficiently. She is a figure of self-respect and self-care and she says, with unspoken words, that if you want to live happily, you have to live true to yourself. <br /><br />And as to paraphrase a quote from the book as my own strong impression of Circe, <b>however gold she shines, do not forget her fire.</b><br /><br /><br />(<a href="https://urcdn.eu/styles/ad968102-ab78-4603-9c55-a39f5b872bd5_original?b" rel="nofollow noopener">Book-inspired</a>)</span>
  <a data-text-id="1555526946058307871" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2370091652" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2370091652" />
    <a title="Will Byrnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes"><img alt="Will Byrnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291462190p2/1526851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2370091652?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Byrnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Byrnes" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes">Will Byrnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=feminist-dystopian-and-not">feminist-dystopian-and-not</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=books_of_the_year-2018">books_of_the_year-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2370091652" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7772024432091244049">Men, can’t live with ‘em, can’t turn ‘em all into swine. <br/><br/>What do you mean <i>turn them into </i> swine? From her earliest application of her new found transformative skills it is suggested that what Circe turns her unfortunate guests into has more to do with their innermost nature than Circe’s selection of a target form. (<i>The strength of those flowers lay in their sap, which could transform any creature to its truest self.</i>) Clearly her sty residents had an oinky predisposition. And I am sure that there</span>
  <span id="freeText7772024432091244049" style="display:none">Men, can’t live with ‘em, can’t turn ‘em all into swine. <br><br>What do you mean <i>turn them into </i> swine? From her earliest application of her new found transformative skills it is suggested that what Circe turns her unfortunate guests into has more to do with their innermost nature than Circe’s selection of a target form. (<i>The strength of those flowers lay in their sap, which could transform any creature to its truest self.</i>) Clearly her sty residents had an oinky predisposition. And I am sure that there are many who had started the transformation long before landing on her island.<br><br>Whaddya call the large sty Circe filled with erstwhile men? A good start.<br><br>Ok. You had to know this would be part of the deal for this review. So, now that I have gotten it out of my system, (it is out, right?) we can proceed.<blockquote>
  <b>When I was born, the name for what I was did not exist.</b>
</blockquote>It was a word that Barbara Bush might have had in mind when she described Geraldine Ferraro, her husband’s opponent for the Vice Presidency, in 1984. “"I can't say it, but it rhymes with 'rich,'" she said, later insisting that the word in question did not begin with a “b,” but a “w.” Sure, whatever. But in this case, I suppose both might apply. Circe is indeed the first witch in western literature. And many a sailing crew might have had unkind things to say about her. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524634582i/25429976._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Madeline Miller</b> - image from <i>The Times</i><br><br>Our primary introduction to Circe (which we pronounce as Sir-Sea, and even Miller goes along with this, so people don’t throw things at her. But for how it might be pronounced in Greek, you know, the <i>proper</i> way, you might check out <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rXW5wFQDges" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">this link</a>. Put that down, there will be no throwing of things in this review!) was that wondrous classic of Western literature, <i>The Odyssey</i>. Given how many times this and its companion volume, <i>The Iliad</i>, have been reworked through the ages, it is no surprise that there have been many variations on the stories they told. Circe’s story has seen its share of re-imaginings as well. But Miller tries to stick fairly close to the Homeric version. Be warned, though, <i>some</i> license was taken, and other sources inspired the work as well. But it is from Homer that <i>we</i> get the primary association we have with her name, the magical transmutation of men into pigs.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1445259977i/16622146._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>George Romney's 1782 portrait of Emma Hamilton as Circe</b> - image from wikipedia<br><br>We follow the life of our Ur-witch from birth to whatever. She did not start out with much by way of godly powers. Her mother, Perse, daughter of the sea-god Oceanos, was a nymph, and her father was Helios, the sun god. Despite the lofty position of Pop’s place in things, Circe was just a nymph, on the low end of the godly powers scale. This did not help in the family to which she had been born. Not one of her parents’ favorites, she was blessed with neither power nor beauty, had a very ungod-like human-level voice, and her sibs were not exactly the nicest. Kinda tough to keep up when daddy is the actual bloody sun. <br><br>Years pass, and one day she comes across a mortal fisherman. He seems pretty nice, someone she can talk to. She’d like to take it to the next stage, so she lays low, listens in on family gatherings, and picks up intel on substances that might be used to effect powerful and advantageous changes. She asks her grandmother, Tethys, (very Lannisterish wife AND SISTER to Oceanos) to transform him into a god for her, but Granny throws her out, alarmed when her granddaughter mentions this <i>pharmakos</i> stuff she had been looking into. Left to her own devices she tries this out on her bf, making him into his truest self. It does not end the way she’d hoped. (Pearls before you-know-what.) Not the last bad experience she would have with a man. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524724127i/25434883._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Levy’s 1889 Circe</b> - image from wikipedia<br><br>Her relationships with men are actually not <i>all</i> bad. Daddy is singularly unfeeling, and can be pretty dim for such a bright bulb, and her brothers are far less than wonderful, but there is some good in her sibling connections as well. She has a warm interaction with a titan, Prometheus, which is a net positive. Later, she has an interesting relationship with Hermes, who is <i>not to be trusted,</i> but who offers some helpful guidance. And then there are the mortals, Daedalus (the master artist, the Michelangelo, the Leonardo da Vinci of his era), Jason, of Argonaut fame, Odysseus, who you may have heard of, and more. There were dark encounters as well, and thus the whole turning-men-into-pigs thing. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524724195i/25434886._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Brewer's 1892 Circe and Her Swine</b> - image from Wikipedia<br><br>Miller has had a passion for the classics since she was eight, when her mother read her the <i>Iliad</i> and began taking her to Egyptian and Greek exhibits at the Metropolitan Museum of Art in New York City. It made her a nerdy classmate but was a boon when she got to college and was able to find peers who shared her love of the ancient tales. It was this passion that led her to write her first novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/268343520" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Song of Achilles</a>, a reimagining of Achilles relationship with his lover, Patroclus, a delight of a book, a Times bestseller, and winner of the Orange prize. It took her ten years to write her first novel, about seven for this one and the gestation period for number three remains to be seen. She is weighing whether to base it on Shakespeare’s <i>The Tempest</i> or Virgil’s <i>Aeneid</i>. If past is portent, it will be the latter, and should be ready by about 2025.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524735996i/25435467._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Ulysses and Circe, Angelica Kauffmann, 1786.</b> - image from Miller’s site<br><br>The central, driving force in the story is Circe becoming her fullest possible self. (I suppose one might say she made a silk purse from a sow’s ear. I wouldn’t, but some might.)<blockquote>
<b> This is the story of a woman finding her power and, as part of that, finding her voice. She starts out really unable to say what she thinks and by the end of the book, she’s able to live life on her terms and say what she thinks and what she feels.</b> - from the Bookriot interview</blockquote>Most gods are awful sorts, vain, selfish, greedy, careless of the harm they do to others. Circe actually has better inclinations. For instance, when Prometheus is being tortured by the titans for the crime of giving fire to humans, Circe alone is kind to him, bringing him nectar, and talking with him when no one else offers him anything but anger and scorn. She is curious about mortals, and asks him about them, going so far as to cut herself to experience a bit of humanity. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524724266i/25434887._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Carracci's c. 1590 Ulysses and Circe in the Farnese Palace</b> - image from Wikipedia<br><br>Livestock comes in for some attention outside the sty. Turns out Circe’s father has a thing for a well-turned fetlock, so maybe she comes by her affinity for animals of all sorts, albeit in a very different way, quite naturally. Her island is rich with diverse fauna, including some close companions most of us would flee. An early version of Doctor Doolittle?<blockquote>
  <b> Scholars have debated whether Circe’s pet lions are supposed to be transformed men, or merely tamed beasts. In my novel, I chose to make them actual animals, because I wanted to honor Circe’s connection to Eastern and Anatolian goddesses like Cybele. Such goddesses also had power over fierce animals, and are known by the title Potnia Theron, Mistress of the Beasts.</b>
</blockquote> Not be confused with <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=f7h7frRoAPk" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Beastmaster</a><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524732234i/25435297._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Circe and Odysseus. Allessandro Allori, 1560</b> - image from Miller’s site<br><br>While she has her darker side (she does change her nymph love-rival Scylla into a beast of epic proportions, which gets her sent to her room, or in this case, island, and there is that pig thing again) she is also a welcoming hostess on her isle of exile, Aiaia. (Which sounds to me like the palindromic beginning of a lament, Aiaiaiaiaiaiaia, which might feel a bit more familiar with a minor transformation, to oy-oy-oy-oy-oy-oy-oy-oy). I mean, she runs a pretty nifty BnB, with free-roaming wild animals, of both the barnyard and terrifying sort, a steady flow of wayward nymphs sent there by desperate parents in hopes that Circe might transform them into less troublesome progeny, a table with a seemingly bottomless supply of food and drink. And she is more than willing to offer special services to world-class mortals, among others. I mean, after that little misunderstanding with Odysseus about his men, (Pigs? What pigs? What could you possibly mean? Oh, you mean <i>those</i> pigs. Oopsy. How careless of me.) she not only invites everyone to stay for a prolonged vacay, but shacks up with the peripatetic one, offers him instructions on reaching the underworld, suggests ways to get past Scylla and Charybdis, and probably packs bag lunches for him and his crew. She is not all bad. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524724338i/25434894._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Barker's 1889 Circe</b> - image from Wikipedia<br><br>Circe struggles with the mortals-vs-immortals tension. Her mortal voice makes her less frightening to the short-lived ones, allowing her to establish actual relationships with them that a more boombox-voice-level deity might not be able to manage. Of course, it is still quite limiting that even the youngest of her mortal love interests would wither and die while she remained the same age pretty much forever. Knowing that you will see any man you love die is a definite limiting factor. Yet, she manages. She certainly recognizes what a psycho crew the immortals are, even her immediate family, and respects that mortals who gain fame do so by the sweat of their brow or extreme cunning, (even if it is to dark purpose) not their questionable godly DNA. Reinforcing this is her front row seat to the real-housewives tension between the erstwhile global rulers, the Titans, and the relatively new champions of everything there is, the Olympians. I mean, perpetual torture, thunderbolts, ongoing seditious plots, the nurturing of monsters, wholesale slaughter of mortals? She knows a thing or two, because she’s seen a thing or two.<blockquote>
<b> My thoughts about [Circe as caregiver] really start with the gods, who in Greek myth are horrendous creatures. Selfish, totally invested only in their own desires, and unable to really care for anyone but themselves. Circe has this impulse from the beginning to care for other people. She has this initial encounter with Prometheus where she comes across another god who seems to understand that and also who triggers that impulse in her. I wanted to write about what it’s like when you to want to try to be a good person, but you have absolutely no models for that. How do you construct a moral view coming from a completely immoral family?</b> - from Bookriot interview</blockquote><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524728546i/25435096._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Circe Offering the Cup to Odysseus – by John William Waterhouse – 1891</b> - image from Wikimedia<br><br>Of course, there is a pretty straight line between the sort of MCP hogwash Circe had to endure in the wayback and recent events that have been getting so much attention of late <blockquote>
<b>“I wasn’t trying to write Circe’s story in a modern way… I was just trying to be true to her experience in the ancient world.”<br>“It was a very eerie experience. I would put the book away and check the news. The top story was literally the same issue I had just been writing about — sexual assault, abuse, men refusing to allow women to have any power ... I was drawn to the mystery of her character — why is she turning men into pigs?”</b> – from The Times interview</blockquote>There are plenty of classical connections peppered throughout Circe’s tale. Jason and Medea (niece) pop by for a spell. She is summoned to assist in the birthing of the minotaur (nephew) to her seriously nasty sister. She is part of Scylla’s origin story, interacts with Prometheus (cousin), gives shit to Athena, even heads into the briny deep to take a meeting with a huge sea creature (no, not the Kraaken). Hangs with Penelope (her bf’s wife) and Telemachus (bf’s son), and spends a lot of time with Hermes. She definitely had a life, many even, particularly for someone who was ostracized to live on an island.<blockquote>
<b>For Circe, I would say the <i>Odyssey</i> was my primary touch-stone in the sense that that’s where I started building the character. I take character clues directly from Homer’s text, both large and small. I mentioned her mortal-like voice. The lions. The pigs. And then when I get to the Odysseus episode in the book, I follow Homer obviously very closely…</b> - from the BookRiot interview</blockquote><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524728546i/25435097._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>"Circea", #38 in Boccaccio's c. 1365 <i>De Claris Mulieribus</i>, a catalogue of famous women, from a 1474 edition</b> - image from wikipedia<blockquote>
<b> In terms of sources, I used texts from all over the ancient world and a few from the more modern world as well. For Circe herself, I drew inspiration from Ovid’s <i>Metamorphoses</i> and Apollonius of Rhodes’ <i>Argonautica</i>, Vergil’s <i>Aeneid</i>, the lost epic <i>Telegony</i> (which survives only in summary) and myths of the Anatolian goddess Cybele. For other characters, I was inspired by the <i>Iliad</i>, of course, the tragedies (specifically the <i>Oresteia, Medea</i> and <i>Philoctetes</i>), Vergil’s <i>Aeneid</i> again, Tennyson’s <i>Ulysses</i> and Shakespeare’s <i>Troilus and Cressida</i>. Alert readers may note a few small pieces of Shakespeare’s Ulysses in my Odysseus!</b> - from Refinery29 interview</blockquote><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524728546i/25435098._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Circe – by Lorenzo Garbieri</b> - image From Maicar Greek Mythology Link<br><br>Madeline Miller’s Circe is not a lovelorn, lonely heart desperate for connection in her isolation, but a multi-faceted character (not actually a human being, though), with inner seams of the dark and light sort, with family issues that might seem familiar in feel, if not in external content, with sins on her soul, but a desire to do good, and with a curiosity about the world. She may not have been the brightest light in the house of Helios, but she glowed with an inner strength, a capacity for mercy, an appreciation for genius, beauty and talent, and a fondness for pork. This is the epic story of a life lived to the fullest. Circe is an explorer, a lover, a destroyer, and can be a very angry goddess. This transformative figure is our doorway to a very accessible look at the Greek tales which lie at the root of so much of our culture. If you have a decent grounding in western mythology this will offer a delightful refresher. If you do not, it can offer a delightful introduction, and will no doubt spark a desire to root about for more. Madeline Miller may not have a wand with special powers, or transmogrifying potions at her command, but she demonstrates here a power to transform mere readers into fans. <i>Circe</i> is a fabulous read! You will go hog wild for it. Can you pass the hot dogs? <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=b9434BoGkNQ" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">That’s All Folks</a><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524732234i/25435298.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>
  <i>The Sorceress Circe</i>
</b>, oil painting by Dosso Dossi, c. 1530; in the Borghese Gallery, RomeSCALA/Art Resource, New York – image from Britannica<br><br><br>Review posted – 4/27/2018<br><br>Publication date – 4/10/2018<br><br>December 2018 - <i>Circe</i> wins the 2018 Goodreads Choice Award for favorite Fantasy novel of the year<br><br>=============================<b>EXTRA STUFF</b><br><br>Links to the author’s <a href="http://www.madelinemiller.com/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">personal</a>, <a href="https://twitter.com/MillerMadeline" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> and <a href="https://www.facebook.com/MillerMadeline" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">FB</a> pages<br><br><b>Interviews</b><br>----- BookPage – April 10, 2018 - <a href="https://bookpage.com/interviews/22494-madeline-miller#.WtpReMgh3t8" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Madeline Miller – The season of the witch</a> - by Trisha Ping<br>-----Bookriot – April 19, 2018 - <a href="https://bookriot.com/2018/04/19/madeline-miller-interview/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Writing of Gods and Mortals: A Madeline Miller Interview</a> - by Nikki Vanry<br>-----The Times – April 5, 2018 - <a href="https://www.thetimes.co.uk/article/the-magazine-interview-madeline-miller-the-author-of-this-summer-s-must-read-novel-circe-on-why-she-s-rewriting-the-women-of-greek-epics-89mhlqb90" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Magazine Interview: Madeline Miller, author of this summer’s must-read novel, Circe, on seeing history through women’s eyes</a> - by Helena de Bertodano<br><br>NY Times - April 6, 2018 - A lovely profile from the NY Times - <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/04/06/books/madeline-miller-circe-novel.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Circe, a Vilified Witch From Classical Mythology, Gets Her Own Epic</a> - by Alexandra Alter<br><br>My review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/268343520" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Song of Achilles</a><br><br><a href="http://www.gutenberg.org/ebooks/1727" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Odyssey</a> on Gutenberg<br><br>A very nifty, brief, and entertaining summary of <i>The Odyssey</i> can be found on <a href="https://www.shmoop.com/circe/sightings.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Schmoop.com</a>.<br><br>A fitting <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Ob5KzB_EOqc" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">piece of music</a> from Studio Killers <br><br>================================<b>STUFFING</b><br><br>A wonderful piece from Allan Ishac at Medium, on the Russia investigation. - <div id="review_2555921967" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2555921967" />
    <a title="emma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32879029-emma"><img alt="emma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520568130p2/32879029.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2555921967?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="emma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="emma" href="/user/show/32879029-emma">emma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/39836955-circe">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32879029-emma?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32879029-emma?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32879029-emma?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32879029-emma?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32879029-emma?shelf=unpopular-opinion">unpopular-opinion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32879029-emma?shelf=3-stars">3-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32879029-emma?shelf=non-ya">non-ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32879029-emma?shelf=comments">comments</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32879029-emma?shelf=eh">eh</a>
          </div>
