        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2555921967" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14410324194804727743">It is with immense regret and massive trepidation that I must say...<br/><br/>I don’t really get the hype.<br/><br/>I really and truly don’t. Why do you guys like this book so much? I am going to go through one potentially good thing by one ultimately disappointing thing and see if any of them click.<br/><br/><b>1) Because it’s a cool retelling?</b><br/>This is why I was excited for this, as someone who loves retellings. Recycled plots? Old-timey magic and Revenge and mean dads and royalty and dashed love stories that make women turn wi</span>
  <span id="freeText14410324194804727743" style="display:none">It is with immense regret and massive trepidation that I must say...<br /><br />I don’t really get the hype.<br /><br />I really and truly don’t. Why do you guys like this book so much? I am going to go through one potentially good thing by one ultimately disappointing thing and see if any of them click.<br /><br /><b>1) Because it’s a cool retelling?</b><br />This is why I was excited for this, as someone who loves retellings. Recycled plots? Old-timey magic and Revenge and mean dads and royalty and dashed love stories that make women turn witchy? Sign me the hell up! But this wasn’t really a retelling. It felt like if I were like “I’m going to rewrite American history, but from the perspective of some random chick who happened to be there for/sexually involved in a bunch of it.” It didn’t feel like one cohesive story at all. It felt like me writing a series of vignettes about a woman who had a crush on multiple founding fathers.<br /><br /><b>2) Did you all like Percy Jackson?</b><br />Is this Percy Jackson for the now-kind-of-grown-ups who once read Percy Jackson? Were you all the weird 11-year-olds who thought they were Greek mythology professors because they read about Logan Lerman’s quirky sea-based summer camp misadventures? (Sorry I mentioned the movie. I know you guys hate that.) I read those books in elementary school, too, but even then I was extremely cool and quirky and unique and had tons of unpopular opinions and wasn’t a big fan. So maybe it’s that?<br /><br /><b>3) Let’s talk about Greek mythology except with one woman instead of a bunch of men?</b><br />This is another reason I was excited about this, because I cannot think of a single instance in which a single witchy woman is not a million jillion times better than 100 interchangeable White Men Of History Or Fake Magic History. Well, now I can think of a single instance. If that woman spends the whole book basically on one island, even if that island is near monsters, even if that woman gets the occasional visit from a minor god or two, that is so much more boring. Because even with some significant editorializing, that woman is still witnessing maybe 7% of what’s going down. And it’s not really the fun 7%.<br /><br /><b>4) Let’s talk about Greek mythology except make it feminist and take out the girl hate?</b><br />Another thing that I was excited about and then all of the excitement was brutally eked out of my soul over the course of like 400 hours of listening or however long this book was. Circe f*cks a married man or two in this book, and uh. Last time I checked...not the most feminist? Also, she’s not exactly awesome to the spouses of these dudes. Double also, I feel like Penelope (Odysseus’ wife) was a pretty blank slate and could’ve been made into a real badass. Instead she, like so much of this book, was boring as hell.<br /><br /><b>5) Because there’s a badass female protagonist?</b><br />This was like the single biggest selling point to me, and therefore THE MOST WRENCHING DISAPPOINTMENT. I truly feel -- and maybe I’m the only one who feels this way -- like this was not so much a badass female protagonist as a woman who spends an (immortal, neverending) lifetime with a series of men. I did not want a series of romances. I wanted a badass, vengeful woman with witchy magical powers. <br /><br />Bottom line:<br /><br />What I wanted: Feminism! Mythology! Retelling! Badass cool protagonist!<br /><br />What I got: Boring. Island. More island. <br /><br />----------<br /><i>currently-reading updates</i><br /><br />me: ok time to write an essay!<br /><br />this traitor book: or - and here me out on this one - you could not do that and read instead<br /><br />me: hm...compelling</span>
  <a data-text-id="14410324194804727743" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2255066363" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2255066363" />
    <a title="destiny ♡⚔♡ [howling libraries]" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15335689-destiny-howling-libraries"><img alt="destiny ♡⚔♡ [howling libraries]" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557144778p2/15335689.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2255066363?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="destiny ♡⚔♡ [howling libraries]" class="user" itemprop="url" name="destiny ♡⚔♡ [howling libraries]" href="/user/show/15335689-destiny-howling-libraries">destiny ♡⚔♡ [howling libraries]</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781408890080" href="/book/show/32993458-circe">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15335689-destiny-howling-libraries?shelf=owned-physical">owned-physical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15335689-destiny-howling-libraries?shelf=otp">otp</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15335689-destiny-howling-libraries?shelf=witches">witches</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15335689-destiny-howling-libraries?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15335689-destiny-howling-libraries?shelf=review-copies">review-copies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15335689-destiny-howling-libraries?shelf=the-best-of-the-best">the-best-of-the-best</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15335689-destiny-howling-libraries?shelf=owned-ebooks">owned-ebooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2255066363" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8490019604495309081"><blockquote>
  <i> When I was born, the name for what I was did not exist.</i>
</blockquote><br/>Where do I even begin? This was one of the most amazing, beautiful, intricate, captivating books I have had the pleasure of reading in my entire life. I have been a bookworm since I was barely walking, and yet <i>this</i> book, this gorgeous retelling, has impacted me so profoundly that I genuinely do not know if I will ever be entirely the same.<br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>It is a common saying that women are delicate creatures—flowers, eggs, anything that may be crus</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText8490019604495309081" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i> When I was born, the name for what I was did not exist.</i>
</blockquote><br />Where do I even begin? This was one of the most amazing, beautiful, intricate, captivating books I have had the pleasure of reading in my entire life. I have been a bookworm since I was barely walking, and yet <i>this</i> book, this gorgeous retelling, has impacted me so profoundly that I genuinely do not know if I will ever be entirely the same.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>It is a common saying that women are delicate creatures—flowers, eggs, anything that may be crushed in a moment’s carelessness. If I had ever believed it, I no longer did.</i>
</blockquote><br />As a child, I loved Greek mythology, and though I lost some of that knowledge through recent years, when I heard that this story was releasing, I knew I just had to read it. I thought it was going to be the story from Circe’s point of view, but ultimately, I expected it to revolve around Odysseus; I had no idea that I was in for such a treat, though, as he is only a small portion of the immortal Circe’s life. This isn’t a retelling, it’s an origin story, a history, a tale of centuries’ worth of loves and losses, griefs and triumphs.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>The thought was this: that all my life had been murk and depths, but I was not a part of that dark water. I was a creature within it.</i>
</blockquote><br />From the very start, we see that Circe is so vastly set apart from her fellow gods and goddesses; as a nymph with the reedy voice of a mortal, she is told she is wholly useless, but it’s evident from the beginning that she is this brilliant, clever, strong woman: a force to be reckoned with in every way. I knew I would love her, but I couldn’t have predicted how fast or hard I would find myself rooting for her to succeed.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i> But of course I could not die. I would live on, through each scalding moment to the next. This is the grief that makes our kind choose to be stones and trees rather than flesh.</i>
</blockquote><br />Of course, Circe’s exile on the isle of Aiaia is bound to be an unhappy story, and that’s a common thread throughout <i>Circe</i>: you always know something miserable or painful is on its way, but the moments in between those travesties, and the ways Circe handles the hand of cards life has dealt her, makes it so incredibly worth the ache. Perhaps the greatest thing about watching her struggle is how much relatability it lends to her character; despite being a goddess, an immortal, and a witch, Circe at her core is a spurned woman who has lived too long under the heels of spiteful, power-hungry men, and a wicked society that values beauty over strength.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i> But in a solitary life, there are rare moments when another soul dips near yours, as stars once a year brush the earth. Such a constellation was he to me.</i>
</blockquote><br />Of course, Circe’s tale is not entirely a desolate one, but her joys are often her curses, as she loves mortals and sees in them the same potential that cursed Prometheus to his rock. Throughout her life, we get to see relationships come and go, and I was enthralled by how incredibly sex-positive and sure of herself she remains. Rather than selling herself away to the highest bidder, Circe’s primary focus is to never let her pursuit of pleasures and companionship win out over her need to be her own person.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i> “It is not fair,” I said. “I cannot bear it.”<br />“Those are two different things,” my grandmother said.</i>
</blockquote><br />It was so enjoyable to watch the different characters cycle in and out of her memories, whether it was Daedalus and his loom, or Hermes and his messages and antics, or—of course—Odysseus, who we saw in a much more realistic light, as Circe portrayed an image of him that was far less heroic or noble than many of the legends would have one believe. There are even mentions of Patroclus and Achilles, and what became of them, though I was pleased to find that prior knowledge of <i>The Song of Achilles</i> was not at all necessary to fully enjoy this book.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i> I would look at him and feel a love so sharp it seemed my flesh lay open. I made a list of all the things I would do for him. Scald off my skin. Tear out my eyes. Walk my feet to bones, if only he would be happy and well.</i>
</blockquote><br />Of all the things Madeline’s writing had to offer me, though, the one that meant the most to me was wholly unexpected: the perfect, beautiful depiction of motherhood through Circe’s relationship with her son. As a mother to a wild little boy of my own, I related to so many of her thoughts and fears, but most of all, to the utter authenticity of the love she describes for him. It consumes her entirely—for better or for worse—and her need to protect him holds such ferocity that she worries it will destroy her at times. Many of the thoughts she held for him gave me chills or brought tears to my eye, and throughout it all, I just kept thinking that I had <i>never</i> felt like motherhood had been so perfectly described as it is in this book.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i> You threw me to the crows, but it turns out I prefer them to you.</i>
</blockquote><br />Truly, I could gush for days, but I’m going to cut myself off here and just ask you to please, <i>please</i> pick up a copy of this beautiful book. I sound like a broken record, but it meant so much to me, and has earned such a warm place in my heart that I know I will reread it over and over in the coming years. Whether you are a mother, or a lover of Greek mythology, or just a bookworm looking for a story that will capture you so wholly, you’ll never want to leave its embrace—this book is flawless, utter perfection, and I cannot possibly recommend it highly enough.<br /><br /><i>All quotes come from an unfinished ARC and may not match the finished release. Thank you to Little, Brown and Company for providing me with an ARC in exchange for my honest review!</i><br /><br />Buddy read with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/44071425-heather-the-sassy-book-geek" rel="nofollow noopener">Heather</a>! <br /><br />You can find this review and more on my <a href="https://howlinglibraries.com/2018/04/02/circe/" rel="nofollow noopener">blog</a>, or you can follow me on <a href="https://twitter.com/howlinglibrary" rel="nofollow noopener">twitter</a>, <a href="https://instagram.com/howlinglibraries" rel="nofollow noopener">bookstagram</a>, or <a href="https://facebook.com/howlinglibraries" rel="nofollow noopener">facebook</a>!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8490019604495309081" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2337044153" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2337044153" />
    <a title="Felice Laverne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50404233-felice-laverne"><img alt="Felice Laverne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548611171p2/50404233.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2337044153?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Felice Laverne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Felice Laverne" href="/user/show/50404233-felice-laverne">Felice Laverne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50404233-felice-laverne?shelf=supernatural">supernatural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50404233-felice-laverne?shelf=full-review">full-review</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50404233-felice-laverne?shelf=read-2018">read-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50404233-felice-laverne?shelf=reviewed-on-amazon">reviewed-on-amazon</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50404233-felice-laverne?shelf=lit-fic">lit-fic</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2337044153" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2460780782959752202"><b>
  <i>3.5 stars</i>
</b><br/><br/>Madeline Miller’s <i>Circe</i> is an epic that’s sweeping the nation today. Everywhere you turn, you see that magnificent cover (honestly, that cover work is DIVINE and I’ve had the MOST fun photographing it for the Bookstagram). Twitter and Instagram are as we speak packed with <i>Circe</i> references and Miller interviews and, within all of that, <i>Circe</i> has found itself wrapped in all of the fluff and buildup and publicity of a typical ultra-hyped, big-named publisher release. Let’s be serious—m</span>
  <span id="freeText2460780782959752202" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>3.5 stars</i>
</b><br /><br />Madeline Miller’s <i>Circe</i> is an epic that’s sweeping the nation today. Everywhere you turn, you see that magnificent cover (honestly, that cover work is DIVINE and I’ve had the MOST fun photographing it for the Bookstagram). Twitter and Instagram are as we speak packed with <i>Circe</i> references and Miller interviews and, within all of that, <i>Circe</i> has found itself wrapped in all of the fluff and buildup and publicity of a typical ultra-hyped, big-named publisher release. Let’s be serious—most of us <b>LOVE</b> these kinds of releases and all the hysteria involved, even if we shy away from actually reading the hyped release itself. It can be a book lover’s dream--a book with all the fanfare of a blockbuster, silver screen release. I, too, was swept up in the craze, yet another smash hit from the publisher who brought us fan favorites like <i>Twilight</i> and whom I once interned for in London. But, in the end, I couldn’t ride the <i>Circe</i> wave all the way through. <br /><br />Let’s get one thing clear from the very start: Madeline Miller’s follow up to <i>The Song of Achilles</i> is an epic in the years spanned but not necessarily in the execution. To me, it read far more like a long story than an “epic.” When I think of that four-lettered word, I think of a novel that’s monumental and moving. I think of <i>The Odyssey</i> and sweeping sagas like <i>A Song of Ice and Fire</i>, even novels that are gripping and complex, long and treacherous as a Hajj like <i>A Little Life</i>. But <i>Circe</i> did not touch me in that way. In fact, there were moments—those times when the novel resorted to recounting the tales of the mythology we all know so well rather than <s>putting the reader <i>in</i> the moment of these tales</s>—where I was bored to skimming. In <i>Circe</i>, pages upon pages passed of one character telling another a “story” of others’ happenings, travels and wars: Telemachus telling Circe about Odysseus, Circe recounting the story of Hermes, and on and on. Who wants to hear second- and third-hand about the chronicles of these larger-than-life names within a novel that calls itself an “epic?” I’d rather <b>feel and live</b> the stories of these mortals and gods alike. Wouldn’t you?<br /><br />Circe’s life is a true saga, and Miller’s research and background in the Classics shines through in this novel and serves her well. But, there are gaps between the breadth and notoriety of the mythology she incorporated here and the skill with which the novel was actually written. There is only so far that building a modern-day epic on the backs of known names and legends could go; Miller needed to take us the rest of the way to make <i>Circe</i> a contemporary wonder, to make this a saga all her own. This novel didn’t quite make it across that bridge for me.<br /><br /><i>Circe</i> offered up a world full of color, a world of eternal life and leviathans, of clashing gods and witchcraft, all while tying in mythological tales that’ve been handed down for ages. Perhaps it’s only fitting, in that case, that it was overwrought with linguistic hyperbole—<i>The sound was a piercing chaos, like a thousand dogs howling at once…She beat the cliff-side, howling her frustration</i>. This novel was full of both drama and melodrama, only one of which is necessary for a sweeping epic. Yet, I had to appreciate the scale of story Miller told and the breadth of her knowledge in the Classics. <i>Circe</i> was a great story for sure, but I was never fully moved by how it was told. 3.5 stars.*** <br /><br /><b>FOLLOW ME HERE:</b><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/49185491-the-other-americans?ac=1&amp;from_search=true&amp;qid=tl37nwckP6&amp;rank=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Goodreads</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/queenbee_fifi" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="https://www.instagram.com/thefelicelaverne/" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://www.amazon.com/Other-Americans-Novel-Felice-Laverne/dp/1672094488/ref=sr_1_1?crid=25JA8C7GHLFY8&amp;keywords=the+other+americans+felice+laverne&amp;qid=1579649731&amp;sprefix=the+other+americans+felice%2Caps%2C208&amp;sr=8-1" rel="nofollow noopener">Get a Copy of My Book</a> | <a href="https://www.artanddecoagency.org" rel="nofollow noopener">Book Editing, Author Coaching, Submit Your Book to Me</a> </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2383798027" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2383798027" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten"><img alt="Jeffrey Keeten" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2383798027?book_show_action=true">May 12, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Keeten" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=greek">greek</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=myth">myth</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2383798027" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8141534554865988313"><b>”It was true what Hermes said. Every moment mortals died, by shipwreck and sword, by wild beasts and wild men, by illness, neglect, and age. It was their fate, as Prometheus had told me, the story that they all shared. No matter how vivid they were in life, no matter how brilliant, no matter the wonders they made, they came to dust and smoke. Meanwhile every petty and useless god would go on sucking down the bright air until the stars went dark.”</b><br/><br/><a href='https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Prometheus_Adam_Louvre_zpslp4dgqgt.jpg.html' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1532703771i/26037906._SY540_.jpg' alt=' photo Prometheus_Adam_Louvre_zpslp4dgqgt.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><b>Sculpture by Nicolas-Sébastien Adam in the Lou</b></span>
  <span id="freeText8141534554865988313" style="display:none"><b>”It was true what Hermes said. Every moment mortals died, by shipwreck and sword, by wild beasts and wild men, by illness, neglect, and age. It was their fate, as Prometheus had told me, the story that they all shared. No matter how vivid they were in life, no matter how brilliant, no matter the wonders they made, they came to dust and smoke. Meanwhile every petty and useless god would go on sucking down the bright air until the stars went dark.”</b><br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Prometheus_Adam_Louvre_zpslp4dgqgt.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1532703771i/26037906._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo Prometheus_Adam_Louvre_zpslp4dgqgt.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Sculpture by Nicolas-Sébastien Adam in the Louvre.</b><br><br>Meeting Prometheus in chains, very briefly, before he was taken to the mountain side to begin his punishment had a profound impact on Circe. He had given man fire, and in the process had angered the Gods. He was condemned by Zeus to have an eagle rip his liver from his body each day and eat it over and over again for all eternity. Mortals paid attention to the Gods more when they experienced more suffering. Fire reduced their offerings to the Gods. One might say that fire made them need the Gods less. <br><br><b>Gods are fickle, childish creatures in need of constant reassurance.</b> <br><br>Circe was a daughter of Helios. <b>”At my father’s feet, the whole world was made of gold. The light came from everywhere at once, his yellow skin, his lambent eyes, the bronze flashing of his hair. His flesh was hot as a brazier, and I pressed as close as he would let me, like a lizard to noonday rocks. My aunt had said that some of the lesser gods could scarcely bear to look at him, but I was his daughter and blood, and I stared at his face so long that when I looked away it was pressed upon my vision still, glowing from the floor, the shining walls and inlaid tables, even my own skin.”</b><br><br>She was the oldest daughter of the nymph Perse, and she was quickly followed by three siblings. When Zeus discovered they were all witches, he ordered Helios to slake his lust elsewhere. Maybe that was when Helios started turning himself into a bull and fucking his herd of precious cows. I’m not sure if that was bestiality or if it deserved some new moniker to describe such perversity. <br><br><blockquote>
  <a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Circe_zpsxwbo0s0j.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1532703771i/26037907._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo Circe_zpsxwbo0s0j.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  </a>
  <br>
  <b>Painting of Circe by Joseph Herrin</b>
</blockquote><br><br>Circe could never win the approval of her father because she was simply not as beautiful as she should be. Her voice was too thin, like a mortals, and her chin was too sharply made. When I looked at a picture of the Roosevelt family with all those attractive features, broad shoulders, and waspish waists, Eleanor Roosevelt stood out. She was Circe amongst all that beauty. In a normal family, attractive attributes could be noticed about Eleanor, but standing in the midst of the Roosevelts she was a flower with too few petals. <br><br>Circe’s siblings and cousins made her life a godly hell. They lived forever, and spite and vindictiveness were the slings and arrows of idle hands. She was lonely and made more lonely by the fact that no God would marry her, and mortals were simply not good enough for the daughter of Helios. <br><br>She was discovering that she had powers. The very witchcraft that made the Gods shift uneasily in their thrones. She could transform an iris into a rose or a bee into a mouse. Then she met the mortal fisherman Glaucos. What she does to him confirmed all the fears that the Olympians had trembled over before. Her powers were a wellspring not yet beginning to geyser. <br><br>Oh, and she turned the bitchy Scylla into a more representative version of herself. <br><br><b>*Shudder*.</b><br><br>Circe was banished to the island of Aiaia. <br><br><b>*Sigh*.</b> Perpetually misunderstood. <br><br>I liked the way Madeline Miller tied in Circe’s encounter with Prometheus, who sacrificed eternal torment for humanity, and what would turn out to be her lifetime fascination with mortals. Chicks dig scars, and Circe was no exception. After growing up with Gods whose skin, despite what hazards are encountered, remained unblemished, those scars on mortals were fascinating to her because they told the story of their lives in every livid slash and puncture. They might have worn their scars on their skin, but Circe bore hers on her soul. She wanted to help mortals, but found that usually when she tried to help, she made things worse. Not that there weren’t bobbles in her relationship with mortals. After all, she did spend many years turning them into pigs, but then she was only bringing to light the least attractive part of their inner selves. <br><br>She may have loved the mortal Odysseus the most, which brought her into conflict with: <b>”She struck the room, tall and straight and sudden-white, a talon of lightning in the midnight sky. Her horse-hair helmet brushed the ceiling. Her mirror armor threw off sparks. The spear in her hand was long and thin, its keen edge limned in firelight. She was burning certainty, and before her all the shuffling and strained dross of the world must shrink away. Zeus’ bright and favorite child, Athena.”</b><br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/odysseus_zps5jvgbi4r.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1532703771i/26037908.jpg" alt=" photo odysseus_zps5jvgbi4r.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Odysseus</b><br><br>Odysseus might have been the cleverest man of his generation, but Circe would have had to be even more clever as she harnessed what power she had to outwit a God that wished to have Odysseus at all cost, but also wished to bring harm to Circe’s son, as well. <br><br>A wonderful, reimagining of an ancient tale that was deftly brought to life by the assured, clear, precise writing style of a gifted writer and researcher. Don’t tarry any longer. Experience the pleasure of epic triumph and tragedy spun in the threads of Daedalus’s loom and wrapped in the magic that only Circe could conjure. <br><br>If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br>I also have a Facebook blogger page at:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="8141534554865988313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2317968182" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2317968182" />
    <a title="Emily (Books with Emily Fox)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58160628-emily-books-with-emily-fox"><img alt="Emily (Books with Emily Fox)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546189365p2/58160628.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2317968182?book_show_action=true">Apr 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily (Books with Emily Fox)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily (Books with Emily Fox)" href="/user/show/58160628-emily-books-with-emily-fox">Emily (Books with Emily Fox)</a>
      </span>

        rated it

<div id="review_3212976664" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3212976664" />
    <a title="Petrik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59458347-petrik"><img alt="Petrik" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1568016775p2/59458347.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3212976664?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Petrik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Petrik" href="/user/show/59458347-petrik">Petrik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/50164163-circe">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Greek mythology fans</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3212976664" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7913110698019704397"><b>4.5/5 stars<br/><br/>Madeline Miller is now on my must-read author list. I can’t wait for her next work already.</b><br/><br/>I guess I can officially say with no reservation that I’m a fan of Madeline Miller’s books now. Many readers have raved about her books for almost a decade now, compared to them, I definitely can be considered a new fan of Madeline Miller. I finished reading <i>The Song of Achilles</i> almost two months ago, and despite my previous hesitancy—I talked about why in my review of the book—to read that book</span>
  <span id="freeText7913110698019704397" style="display:none"><b>4.5/5 stars<br /><br />Madeline Miller is now on my must-read author list. I can’t wait for her next work already.</b><br /><br />I guess I can officially say with no reservation that I’m a fan of Madeline Miller’s books now. Many readers have raved about her books for almost a decade now, compared to them, I definitely can be considered a new fan of Madeline Miller. I finished reading <i>The Song of Achilles</i> almost two months ago, and despite my previous hesitancy—I talked about why in my review of the book—to read that book, it blew me away how good it was. Right upon finishing it, I knew I had to give <i>Circe</i> a read as well, and although I slightly loved <i>The Song of Achilles</i> more, I cannot deny that <i>Circe</i> is another incredible book by Madeline Miller.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>“It is a common saying that women are delicate creatures, flowers, eggs, anything that may be crushed in a moment's carelessness. If I had ever believed it, I no longer did.” </blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br /><br />When I was starting <i>The Song of Achilles</i>, I was afraid that my knowledge of Achilles and the Trojan War would diminish my experience on Miller’s take on the story. As it turns out, knowing about Achilles and how his story ended actually deepened my enjoyment of reading that book. When I was going to start <i>Circe</i>, it was the other way around; I knew about Odysseus and his journey, but I admittedly remembered very little about Circe’s tale. In fact, what I remembered about Circe was only that Odysseus met her, and she also turned Odysseus’ men into pigs, that’s it. I was afraid that my lack of knowledge about Circe would actually decrease my enjoyment; there was no need for any worry, after all. <i>Circe</i> is beautiful, empowering, and well-written.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote> “Humbling women seems to me a chief pastime of poets. As if there can be no story unless we crawl and weep.” </blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br /><br />Circe’s characterization is simply wonderful. It was easy for me to find myself invested in her story, and the more I read about Miller’s take on her character, the more I grew to care about her. She’s a kind-hearted and innocent individual who has to learn things the hard way but never let the difficulties, betrayals, and loneliness she faced throughout her life changed her core virtues. Her character’s development was gradually developed. This is also what made Circe such a compelling character to read; she’s powerful, and I’m speaking this not just in terms of her literal power as a witch, but it’s her perseverance, defiance, and strong mentality that I found to be inspiring. The cruel events that have happened to Circe could’ve easily led her to think that all male is evil, but this didn’t happen to her; Circe judged people, regardless of their genders and affiliations, equally through their actions. Good people receive kindness, bad people deserve retribution.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote> “You have always been the worst of my children,” he said. “Be sure to not dishonor me.”<br />“I have a better idea. I will do as I please, and when you count your children, leave me out.” </blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br /><br />Greek mythology—along with Norse and Japanese mythologies—are some of my favorite myths to read about. I loved reading Miller’s Greek retellings. After reading <i>The Song of Achilles</i>, it somehow felt comfortable to me to be reading another standalone story by Miller within these eras. It was great to witness Scylla and its origin; Prometheus and his torture; Daedalus and Icarus; Odysseus’ and his family; the Greek gods behaving as childish as possible, and many more. These, and heroic actions, are all the kinds of things that made Greek mythologies fascinating to read, and Miller continues to nail the executions. The part with the Trygon’s Tail was something that I haven’t heard of; this could be Miller’s own rendition on this story section, and it fits the narrative she tells.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote> “You cannot know how frightened gods are of pain. There is nothing more foreign to them, and so nothing they ache more deeply to see.” </blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br /><br />Both <i>The Song Achilles</i> and Circe has proved that Madeline Miller is a blessing for literature and Greek mythologies. Feel free to consider me a fan of her books now, I heard that her next novel will be about Pandora, and I’m super excited for it. Honestly speaking, though, I loved reading Miller’s beautiful prose so much that I don’t think I’d mind if she decides to retell everything in Greek mythologies with her creativity and writing.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote> “But in a solitary life, there are rare moments when another soul dips near yours, as stars once a year brush the earth. Such a constellation was he to me.” </blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br /><br /><b>You can order the book from: <u><a href="http://www.bookdepository.com/book/9781408890080/?a_aid=petrikleo" rel="nofollow noopener">Book Depository (Free shipping)</a></u></b><br /><br /><b>You can find <u><a href="http://novelnotions.net/2020/05/19/book-review-circe-by-madeline-miller-2/" rel="nofollow noopener">this</a></u> and the rest of my reviews at <u><a href="http://novelnotions.net" rel="nofollow noopener">Novel Notions</a></u></b><br /><br /><i>Special thanks to my Patrons on <b><u><a href="https://www.patreon.com/petrikleo" rel="nofollow noopener">Patreon</a></u></b> for giving me extra support towards my passion for reading and reviewing!<br /><br />My Patrons: Alfred, Devin, Hamad, Joie, Mike, Miracle, Nicholas.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="7913110698019704397" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3061697333" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3061697333" />
    <a title="Virginia Ronan ♥ Herondale ♥" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/69106439-virginia-ronan-herondale"><img alt="Virginia Ronan ♥ Herondale ♥" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577855357p2/69106439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3061697333?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Virginia Ronan ♥ Herondale ♥" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Virginia Ronan ♥ Herondale ♥" href="/user/show/69106439-virginia-ronan-herondale">Virginia Ronan ♥ Herondale ♥</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781408890042" href="/book/show/41045102-circe">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3061697333" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2360406238311900902"><i>”Of all the mortals on the earth, there are only a few the gods will ever hear of. Consider the practicalities. By the time we learn their names, they are dead. They must be meteors indeed to catch our attention. The merely good: you are dust to us."</i><br/><br/>I thought long and hard what to write about <b>“Circe”</b> but when it comes down to it the first words that always pop into my mind are: “I loved this so damn much!!!” *lol* Which is neither especially eloquent nor particularly significant. So what to do? </span>
  <span id="freeText2360406238311900902" style="display:none"><i>”Of all the mortals on the earth, there are only a few the gods will ever hear of. Consider the practicalities. By the time we learn their names, they are dead. They must be meteors indeed to catch our attention. The merely good: you are dust to us."</i><br /><br />I thought long and hard what to write about <b>“Circe”</b> but when it comes down to it the first words that always pop into my mind are: “I loved this so damn much!!!” *lol* Which is neither especially eloquent nor particularly significant. So what to do? How to describe my love for this book in words that would do this masterpiece (and yes, in my opinion it truly is a masterpiece) justice? <br /><br /><i>”I felt myself tremble, but I would not let him see it. Great gods smell fear like sharks smell blood, and they will devour you for it just the same.”</i><br /><br />I guess I might start with what it is not. “Circe” is definitely not like “The Song of Achilles”! To be honest those books couldn’t have been any more different even if they tried. Whilst the main topics of “The Song of Achilles” were the Trojan War and the undying love *weeps* between Patroclus and Achilles the major themes in “Circe” were her solitary life in exile and her encounter with Odysseus. This setting alone makes for an entirely different story and sets a completely different pace. The tale of Circe is slow and unfolds over time, the many centuries that pass away giving the reader not only a good sense of the change of the world but also an insight to our heroine’s lonely life. Just like the love between Patroclus and Achilles was almost tangible in every single page from “The Song of Achilles” Circe’s solitude is palpable in every written line. <br /><br /><i>”I had no right to claim him, I knew it. But in a solitary life, there are rare moments when another soul dips near yours, as stars once a year brush the earth. Such a constellation was he to me.”</i><br /><br />The remarkable thing about her is that she didn’t let it get her down. She might have been in exile and shunned by her family but she never got bitter because of it. Quite the contrary. She took what she had and changed it according to her needs and she adapted to her new life and made the best out of it. And even more important, she defied the gods by doing so. It might have been small gestures and little actions but with every breath she took she refused to give them any satisfaction. Circe was a strong-willed, skilled and formidable woman and I couldn’t help but love her for it! <br /><br /><i>”We lit no tapers. The room was dark and warm from the day's heat. Shadows draped the bed. No frogs sounded, no birds called. It was as if we had found the still heart of the universe. Nothing moved except for us."</i><br /><br />Despite her bravery and strong will it’s undeniable that she was a very lonely woman though. This however doesn’t mean that she didn’t know love. There were quite a few lovers and mortals she didn’t only take to her bed but also into her heart and whenever she did it; it left a mark on her soul. Circe loved deeply and gently and she always felt compassion for those men that came to her island and crossed her path. Well, at least until one day that changed everything. <br /><br /><i>”They moaned and squealed, and pressed their snouts to the earth. We are sorry, we are sorry.<br />Sorry you were caught, I said. Sorry that you thought I was weak, but you were wrong.”</i><br /><br />And tell you what? I could understand her anger and wrath, because I think this was the first time she truly realized what had been done to her. The gods didn’t intervene on her behalf and left her to her own devices instead and the trust she had given to those who had needed her help was betrayed in the worst possible way. Add to that her acute loneliness and you get a goddess that becomes embittered and vindictive. <br /><br /><i>”And so,” he said, “which do you change, and which do you let go?”<br />“I change them all,” I said. “They have come to my house. Why should I care what is in their hearts?”<br />He had smiled and lifted his cup to me. “Lady, you and I are in accord.”</i><br /><br />Who knows what would have happened if Odysseus would have never come to her island? Which kind of person would she have become? I guess we’ll never find out and I’m pretty sure that’s good the way it is. And here comes another scene that brought me immense joy, the moment Circe and Odysseus meet for the very first time. XD Gods, I loved their conversation so much! The tension and the way they tried to gauge each other’s reactions by talking about seemingly simple things! This was so masterfully done, I can’t even! One of the best book moments ever, I swear! &lt;333 <br /><br /><i>”I confess I'm in awe," he said, "they're never so quiet for me. You must have had quite an effect on them."<br />I heard a humming, like before a spell is cast. His gaze was a honed blade. All this had been prologue. As if we were in a play, we stood.</i><br /><br />I’ve always loved Odysseus and I’ve been a huge fan of him ever since I read the Odyssey. I know he is a hero with many flaws and more morally grey than anything else, but this was always the reason why I loved him so much. He’s human, he’s intelligent, skilled, sly, he’s honourable (most of the time) and he knows how to manipulate people in order to get what the wants. Some might say he’s a dangerous man and they’d be completely right with it. So what’s not to love? *lol*<br /><br /><i>”The world was made of mysteries, and I was only another riddle among the millions. I did not answer him, and though he pretended frustration, yet I began to see that it pleased him in some strange way. A door that did not open at his knock was a novelty in its own right, and a kind of relief as well. All the world confessed to him. He confessed to me.”</i><br /><br />Well, anyway! Those two meet and they clash! And yes, I savoured every single moment of it. Their conversations were really intriguing and it’s obvious Odysseus had a notable impact on Circe’s life. In more ways than one, I might add. ;-P Considering the circumstances this actually sounded kind of dirty now. *lol* Never mind my particular choice of words. XD<br /><br /><b>Conclusion:</b><br /><br />It’s undeniable that Madeline Miller has a way with words and no matter which kind of story she tells, she always seems to be able to turn it into gold. I loved how she managed to weave so many different parts of Greek mythology into “Circe’s” tale and I adored that our heroine was a constant part of it all. The pacing of the book might have been very slow, but this didn’t take away any of my enjoyment. Whilst the ending of “The Song of Achilles” made me ugly cry, “Circe’s” ending had an entirely different effect and gave me a sense of contentment and peace. This was definitely another one of Madeline Miller’s masterpieces and all that’s left to say is that I can really recommend it! ;-)<br /><br />P.S: Last but not least I want to thank the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/22314328-aoife---bookish-babbling" rel="nofollow noopener">"amazingly awesome and adorable Aoife"</a> (yes, that's supposed to be an alliteration ;-P) for buddy reading this book with me! &lt;3 I know our lives didn't make it easy to go through with our BR but it was a lot of fun and I hope we'll go for another one soon! XD<br />_______________________________<br /><br />Let’s start the “How many books of my TBR 2019 can I pack into the last 5 weeks of the year” challenge! ;-P <br /><br />The first book is “Circe”! Obviously! *lol* <br />I should have read this gem ages ago but I was procrastinating too much and now I’m trying to catch up! XD Since I loved “The Song of Achilles” I have no doubt that I’ll enjoy this too. Madeline Miller just has a way with words and I can’t wait to dive into this! <br /><br />Hopefully I won’t be as heartbroken as I was after reading TSoA though. &gt;_&lt;<br />Please be gentle with me, Madeline! XD</span>
  <a data-text-id="2360406238311900902" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2368018844" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2368018844" />
    <a title="Helena of Eretz ✰" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27036454-helena-of-eretz"><img alt="Helena of Eretz ✰" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596076376p2/27036454.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2368018844?book_show_action=true">Apr 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Helena of Eretz ✰" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Helena of Eretz ✰" href="/user/show/27036454-helena-of-eretz">Helena of Eretz ✰</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781408890080" href="/book/show/32993458-circe">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27036454-helena-of-eretz?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27036454-helena-of-eretz?shelf=unpopular-opinions">unpopular-opinions</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27036454-helena-of-eretz?shelf=adult">adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2368018844" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18035804256819026006"><i>Upon further reflection, I'm lowering this to three stars...</i><br/><br/>~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~<br/><br/><b>“Humbling women seems to me a chief pastime of poets. As if there can be no story unless we crawl and weep.”</b><br/><br/>So…I didn’t realise that I unintentionally finished this on <b>International Women’s Day</b>. Go me! Talk about <i>perfect timing</i>!!! :P <br/><br/>Let’s just take a moment to appreciate this book’s <b>beauty</b> (<i>photos not mine</i>):<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1552201117i/27183646.jpg' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1552201117i/27183648.jpg' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1552201117i/27183647.jpg' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>As someone who’s studied the classics, I <b>love mythology retellings</b>. And practically al</span>
  <span id="freeText18035804256819026006" style="display:none"><i>Upon further reflection, I'm lowering this to three stars...</i><br><br>~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~<br><br><b>“Humbling women seems to me a chief pastime of poets. As if there can be no story unless we crawl and weep.”</b><br><br>So…I didn’t realise that I unintentionally finished this on <b>International Women’s Day</b>. Go me! Talk about <i>perfect timing</i>!!! :P <br><br>Let’s just take a moment to appreciate this book’s <b>beauty</b> (<i>photos not mine</i>):<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1552201117i/27183646.jpg" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1552201117i/27183648.jpg" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1552201117i/27183647.jpg" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>As someone who’s studied the classics, I <b>love mythology retellings</b>. And practically all of them, really. I’m even talking about the <i>fun ones</i> like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28187.The_Lightning_Thief__Percy_Jackson_and_the_Olympians___1_" title="The Lightning Thief (Percy Jackson and the Olympians, #1) by Rick Riordan" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lightning Thief</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/37760272.Half_Blood__Covenant___1_" title="Half-Blood (Covenant, #1) by Jennifer L. Armentrout" rel="nofollow noopener">Half-Blood</a>, as well as the <i>trashy YA ones</i> like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9681214.The_Goddess_Test__Goddess_Test___1_" title="The Goddess Test (Goddess Test, #1) by Aimee Carter" rel="nofollow noopener">The Goddess Test</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10429067.Sweet_Venom__Medusa_Girls___1_" title="Sweet Venom (Medusa Girls, #1) by Tera Lynn Childs" rel="nofollow noopener">Sweet Venom</a>. Obviously, I adore the classic stories themselves, too, including: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/516650.The_Iliad" title="The Iliad by Homer" rel="nofollow noopener">The Iliad</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77201.The_Odyssey" title="The Odyssey by Homer" rel="nofollow noopener">The Odyssey</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12912.The_Aeneid" title="The Aeneid by Virgil" rel="nofollow noopener">The Aeneid</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3227621.Metamorphoses" title="Metamorphoses by Ovid" rel="nofollow noopener">Metamorphoses</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6328191.Theogony_Works_and_Days" title="Theogony/Works and Days by Hesiod" rel="nofollow noopener">Theogony/Works and Days</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1519.The_Oresteia_Agamemnon__The_Libation_Bearers__The_Eumenides" title="The Oresteia Agamemnon, The Libation Bearers, The Eumenides by Aeschylus" rel="nofollow noopener">The Oresteia: Agamemnon, The Libation Bearers, The Eumenides</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/186678.The_Voyage_of_Argo_The_Argonautica" title="The Voyage of Argo The Argonautica by Apollonius of Rhodes" rel="nofollow noopener">The Voyage of Argo: The Argonautica</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1523.Prometheus_Bound_and_Other_Plays" title="Prometheus Bound and Other Plays by Aeschylus" rel="nofollow noopener">Prometheus Bound and Other Plays</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1473.Medea_and_Other_Plays" title="Medea and Other Plays by Euripides" rel="nofollow noopener">Medea and Other Plays</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1542.The_Three_Theban_Plays_Antigone__Oedipus_the_King__Oedipus_at_Colonus" title="The Three Theban Plays Antigone, Oedipus the King, Oedipus at Colonus by Sophocles" rel="nofollow noopener">The Three Theban Plays: Antigone, Oedipus the King, Oedipus at Colonus</a>. I grew up on the Greek myths and if anyone tells me that a book is <b>Greek mythology-inspired</b>, it’s an instant add to my overflowing TBR. <i>#SorryNotSorry</i><br><br>Moreover, I love <b>villain origin stories</b>, and as Circe is portrayed as one of the many villains in the <i>Odyssey</i> who stood in the way of Odysseus’ voyage home to Ithaca…I thought that this book would be perfect for me. In addition, Madeline Miller is such a <i>highly acclaimed author</i>, following the release of the tragic love story between Achilles and Patroclus in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13516154.The_Song_of_Achilles" title="The Song of Achilles by Madeline Miller" rel="nofollow noopener">The Song of Achilles</a>, which has been recommended to me, time and time again. <br><br>However, it appears that it <i>wasn’t meant to be</i>. No matter how many five-star ratings this book has received…I just don’t think that they were deserved. Now, don’t get me wrong. Miller is a wonderful author, with such a <b>lush writing style</b> that I felt as if I were reading a piece of epic literature. But she made <b>Circe so…boring?</b><br><br>I saw a lot of references to Homer’s <i>Iliad</i>, Homer’s <i>Odyssey</i>, Virgil’s <i>Aeneid</i>, Ovid’s <i>Metamorphoses</i>, Apollonius’ <i>Argonautica</i>, Euripides’ <i>Medea</i>, and even <i>Telegony</i> (one of the lost instalments of the <i>Epic Cycle</i>, about Circe and Odysseus’ son, Telegonus, that we only have a summary of) and I absolutely <i>LOVED</i> how in depth <b>Miller’s research</b> was. I’m in awe, really. The one that really surprised me was her including storylines from <i>Telegony</i>, as most people haven’t even heard of it because of its status as a lost Greek epic. One would presume that she’d only include the more well-known epics, so I really want to give props to the detail that went into this retelling. Bravo!<br><br><i>“I thought: I cannot bear this world a moment longer. Then, child, make another.”</i><br><br><b>Circe</b> is a titaness. A witch, or <i>pharmakis</i>. A mother. A daughter. A sister. A lover. And most importantly, <b>a woman</b>. This is the most <b>feminist interpretation</b> of Greek mythology that I’ve ever come across, and for that, I am truly thankful. Too often, women are the victims or the villains in Greek mythology. <b>Medea</b> is the scorned woman who drives Jason to suicide. <b>Medusa</b> is the evil that Perseus must defeat. <b>Ariadne</b> is the damsel in distress, needing to be saved by Theseus. <b>Helen</b> is the pretty face that “<i>launched a thousand ships</i>”, who leads men like Hector and Achilles to their deaths. <b>Megara</b> is the innocent wife who is killed in a fit of madness by Heracles. <b>Penelope</b> is the faithful wife who awaits Odysseus’ return home. <i>Abused. Raped. Enslaved. Mutilated. Murdered</i>. On and on, countless women are considered to be <b>inferior or insignificant</b>, in comparison with their male counterparts, the so-called <i>heroes</i>. <br><br>Miller goes on to show that Circe isn’t a <b>strong character</b> <i>DESPITE</i> being a woman. She’s a strong character <i>BECAUSE</i> of it. For that reason, and for that reason alone, no matter what my complaints are about this book, I’m grateful that it was written and that I have a copy on my shelves. This is the mythological retelling that <b>needs to be read by all</b>, male or female. Miller <b>humanised</b> an often-overlooked villain and <b>empowered her</b> in such a feministic way. Circe’s embracement of her womanhood is both wonderful and painful, dramatic and tragic. And best of all, Circe is unapologetic for it. <i>What a queen</i>.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1548726007ra/26983494.gif" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In addition, Circe was definitely…<b>Cirsassy</b> at some points: <br><i>“You have always been the worst of my children,” [Helios] said. “Be sure to not dishonour me.” <br>“I have a better idea. I will do as I please, and when you count your children, leave me out.”</i><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1552201117ra/27183645.gif" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><i>“For a hundred generations, I had walked the world drowsy and dull, idle and at my ease. I left no prints, I did no deeds. Even those who had loved me a little did not care to stay. Then I learnt that I could bend the world to my will, as a bow is bent for an arrow. I would have done that toil a thousand times to keep such power in my hands. I thought: this is how Zeus felt when he first lifted the thunderbolt.”</i><br><br>In all honesty, this novel <b>began amazingly</b> and had me entranced. There were so many <i>gods, monsters, and heroes</i> woven into this tale that I recognised. We meet <b>Prometheus</b> and see his punishment for giving fire to the mortals. We see <b>Helios</b> in his divine glory and his white cattle. <b>Daedalus and Icarus</b> also make appearances before they meet their tragic ends. After, there were <b>Scylla and Charybdis</b>, making the waters deadly to all who traversed there. Then, came <b>Pasiphae</b>, the mother of not only <b>Ariadne</b>, but also the <b>Minotaur</b>, who managed to cement herself in history. In addition, there was <b>Aaetes</b>, who also had the gift of witchcraft, but is most notable for fathering <b>Medea</b> – <i>MY GIRL</i>! Many beloved Olympians also grace Circe’s island of Aiaia, including <b>Hermes, Apollo,</b> and <b>Athena</b>. <br><br><i>“The truth is, men make terrible pigs.”</i><br><br>It was near the middle, where the <b>adventure slowed down</b>, that my interest began to wane and I was projected into <b>a reading slump</b>. The best and arguably <i>most iconic scene</i> is when Circe invites men – who are lost at sea – into her home, sheltering and feeding them. While they ask her to kill her pigs for them to eat, she refuses, but does serve them food and wine. After her hospitality, they proceed to thank her by <b>sexually assaulting her</b>, prompting her to <b>transform them into pigs and killing them</b>. The chapter ends in an empowering moment, when she says, “<i>As it turned out, I did kill pigs that night after all</i>.” You go, girl! <br><br>After <b>rejecting her divine relatives</b> and disassociating herself with them, she <b>embraces a simple and quiet life</b> on the island that she was banished to, after her powers of witchcraft threatened Zeus. When <b>Odysseus and his men</b> wander upon her island, I thought that the novel would pick back up, much like the faster pace of the beginning, but that did not seem to be the case. If anything, it <i>slowed down</i> even further. Not only did I not enjoy the <b>characterisations of Medea and Odysseus</b>, but I found her daily routine <b>to be a bore</b>. In fact, I even read more than <i>SEVEN</i> novels in between this time. I was <i>THAT</i> unenthused. <br><br>But the <i>most uninteresting</i> part to me…and I have no idea why…was Circe’s experience with <b>motherhood</b>. I’m not certain if Miller was trying to portray post-partum depression, but I <i>ACTUALLY</i> fell asleep here. It just wasn’t holding my attention. Circe was a pretty <b>awful mother</b> and Telegonus was a <b>terror of a child</b>. Miller said in an interview that “I wanted Circe’s experience of motherhood to speak of things that are often excluded from ancient epics.” Having said that, I wish that Miller <b>hadn’t included it</b>. It’s what <i>dropped this book’s rating</i> from a five-star to a four for me. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1552201117ra/27183649.gif" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>“He showed me his scars, and in return, he let me pretend I had none.”</i><br><br>I was pretty “<i>meh</i>,” about Circe’s <i>casual relationship</i> with Odysseus. I much preferred her <i>pure affection</i> for Daedalus early in the book or her <i>mature bond</i> with Telemachus later in the novel. Miller made my hero, <b>Odysseus, to be utterly reprehensible</b>, and she portrayed my favourite ancient Greek figure, <b>Medea, to be a spoilt brat</b>. How disappointing…<br><br>Regardless, the book turned around in the end, thank goodness. Once Telegonus grew with naïve innocence, bringing Telemachus and Penelope to Aiaia, after having accidentally slain Odysseus, the novel <b>picks back up</b> to its faster pace in the beginning. <b>Penelope</b> finds her own path, <b>Telegonus</b> goes on his own adventure, and <b>Circe and Telemachus</b> travel the world together. The <b>end was extremely satisfying</b> and I was enjoying it so much that I <b>didn’t want it to conclude</b>. In fact, my exact thought upon finishing the novel was, “<i>THAT’S IT?!!!</i>” If only the middle of this novel was of the same quality as the beginning and the end.<br><br>From what I’ve heard, Miller is currently writing a <b>retelling of the <i>Aeneid</i></b>, centred around <b>Dido, the first queen of Carthage</b>, so I’m really excited for that. I’ve never come across Dido in literature, outside of the <i>Aeneid</i> itself, so I <i>can’t wait</i> to see what Miller does with her story. I’m also really hoping for a <i>cameo from Telegonus</i>, as he should be in the general vicinity, as well. So how long do I have to wait? 2020? 2021? Hopefully, not too long!!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="18035804256819026006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2320585274" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2320585274" />
    <a title="Melissa ♥ Dog/Wolf Lover ♥ Martin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17438949-melissa-dog-wolf-lover-martin"><img alt="Melissa ♥ Dog/Wolf Lover ♥ Martin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603831478p2/17438949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<div id="review_2447443521" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2447443521" />
    <a title="Matthew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16254355-matthew"><img alt="Matthew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464987365p2/16254355.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781549117640" href="/book/show/39209765-circe">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=gr_awards_2018">gr_awards_2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=2019">2019</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2447443521" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9843034574355425006">Circe is a fine book. I can easily see why many give it 5 stars. And, I imagine that it is very popular with people who enjoy mythology. I am only going 3 stars on this because, for me, it just really didn’t hold my interest. But, I will say, I am definitely in the minority on this, so you may want to take my opinion with a grain of salt and go ahead a give it a try.<br/><br/>I thought the first half (or so) was great! 5 stars all the way – very engaging, active, fascinating, etc. Learning about Circe’s e</span>
  <span id="freeText9843034574355425006" style="display:none">Circe is a fine book. I can easily see why many give it 5 stars. And, I imagine that it is very popular with people who enjoy mythology. I am only going 3 stars on this because, for me, it just really didn’t hold my interest. But, I will say, I am definitely in the minority on this, so you may want to take my opinion with a grain of salt and go ahead a give it a try.<br /><br />I thought the first half (or so) was great! 5 stars all the way – very engaging, active, fascinating, etc. Learning about Circe’s early “life” and all the people she interacted with was very cool. Many of the events were very familiar to me and I kept having “I didn’t realize Circe was a part of this!” moments. I hope if you do give it a try, you find yourself saying, “AH-HA!” a lot.<br /><br />But, then things came to a screeching halt. About halfway through until there was about 5% of the book left, not much really happened. I kept thinking that the adventure and story was just taking a break and would get going again, but it was more of a permanent break. As I previously mentioned being in the minority when it comes to my feelings on this book, I am sure many will have no clue why I felt this way. But, for me, it just flat-lined.<br /><br />If the book had kept things going the same the whole way through, I would have gone 5 stars. Maybe Circe’s life ran out of 5-star material at a certain point so the author did her best to work with what she had. I will emphasize again that, in this case, I don’t want my 3 stars to deter you. There are other times that I would stress proceeding with caution if I give a book 3 stars, but I don’t feel very passionate about that in this case. Give it a shot and come back and tell me how wrong I am! I hope you do! <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9843034574355425006" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2373406072" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10943028011121591040"><a href='https://diaryofdifference.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Blog</a> | <a href='https://www.facebook.com/diaryofdifference/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Facebook</a> | <a href='https://twitter.com/DiaryDifference' rel='nofollow noopener'>Twitter</a> | <a href='http://innahcrazy.tumblr.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tumblr</a> | <a href='https://www.instagram.com/diaryofdifference/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Instagram</a> | <a href='https://www.pinterest.co.uk/diaryofdifference/pins/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Pinterest</a> | <br/> <br/><b>
  <i>WHEN I WAS BORN, the name for what I was did not exist.<i></i></i>
</b> <br/> <br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1530655869i/25918058._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I was waiting for two whole months to get this book from the library. And I finally had a chance to read Circe from Madeline Miller. A book that everyone was talking about. The only thing you were gonna see on Instagram. Well, here I am – sitting with the cool kids now, I have read this book.<br/><br/>The reason I wanted to read this book wasn’t because I wanted to be part of the cool</span>
  <span id="freeText10943028011121591040" style="display:none"><a href="https://diaryofdifference.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.facebook.com/diaryofdifference/" rel="nofollow noopener">Facebook</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/DiaryDifference" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="http://innahcrazy.tumblr.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Tumblr</a> | <a href="https://www.instagram.com/diaryofdifference/" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://www.pinterest.co.uk/diaryofdifference/pins/" rel="nofollow noopener">Pinterest</a> | <br> <br><b>
  <i>WHEN I WAS BORN, the name for what I was did not exist.<i></i></i>
</b> <br> <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1530655869i/25918058._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I was waiting for two whole months to get this book from the library. And I finally had a chance to read Circe from Madeline Miller. A book that everyone was talking about. The only thing you were gonna see on Instagram. Well, here I am – sitting with the cool kids now, I have read this book.<br><br>The reason I wanted to read this book wasn’t because I wanted to be part of the cool kids. Actually, it was because Greek Mythology has a special place in my heart. See, I was born in Macedonia, a country full of history, and so very close to Greece, where histories and cultures and traditions match and mix.<br><br>When I was in school, our teachers focused hard on history. Especially Roman and Greek Mythology. So yes, I grew up with Homer’s Iliad and Odyssey and yes, I know all the gods out there, what they do, who they married, who their children are.<br><br>I have read about Circe, but I have never given her any meaning, as she is not mentioned a lot in Homer’s works, as you might already know. And then suddenly, there is a book about her life. I had to read it!!!<br><br><b>
  <i>AND I ABSOLUTELY LOVED IT! FROM THE BOTTOM OF MY HEART!</i>
</b><br> <br>This might be my favorite book of 2018!<br> <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1530655869i/25918059._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I enjoyed Madeline’s writing style. It was so explanatory and calm, and soothing, like swimming in nice calm waters. You would just gulp her words as you read, and before you know it, you have read 200 pages.<br><br>Circe, oh Circe! Her character was so well described – such a strong powerful woman. We start with her childhood, to her growing up, and we follow the process of how she learned things the hard way, how she is naive, and then suddenly isn’t, how she discovers the power she holds within, despite everyone else mocking her and saying otherwise. We see how she decides to say no, how she is not afraid to be a rebel, and how she suffers, and loves, and protects, and cares, and survives, and lives!<br><br>You will read a story about the love a mother has toward her child, the love a woman has toward her man, the love a son has towards her mother, the love for freedom, the love for glory…<br><br>If you love Greek Mythology, you will get the chance to say hi to some of your favourite gods, nymphs, titans, monsters – Zeus, Athena, Poseidon, Prometheus, Odysseus and many more which I will fail to reveal.<br><br>I hardly believe that this is a great book for introducing Greek Mythology to new young readers. I also hardly believe that this book will change the thoughts of many people, the way they see things, the way they live, the way they think.<br><br>It was one of my favorite things about him: how he always fought for his chance.<br> <br>There are a lot of side characters that give their own meaning to the story as well, and there is also Odysseus, and at times it feels as this is his story, but in the end you realised that this story belongs to Circe only!<br><br><b>
  <i>Do not listen to your enemy, Odysseus had once told me. Look at them. It will tell you everything.<br><br>I looked. Armed and armored, she was (Athena), from head to foot, helmet, spear, aegis, greaves. A terrifying vision: the goddess of war, ready for battle. But why had she assembled such a panoply against me, who knew nothing of combat? Unless there was something else she feared, something that made her feel somehow stripped and weak.<br><br>Instinct carried me forward, the thousand hours I had spent in my father’s halls, and with Odysseus polymetis, man of so many wiles.</i>
</b><br> <br>To all of you out there – please take your time to read this book! It will leave you breathless, inspired, motivated and it will change your life forever. It changed my life – that’s for certain!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10943028011121591040" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
