        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2354528333?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="☘Misericordia☘ ~ The Serendipity Aegis ~  ⚡ϟ⚡ϟ⚡⛈ ✺❂❤❣" class="user" itemprop="url" name="☘Misericordia☘ ~ The Serendipity Aegis ~  ⚡ϟ⚡ϟ⚡⛈ ✺❂❤❣" href="/user/show/6603759-misericordia-the-serendipity-aegis">☘Misericordia☘ ~ The Serendipity Aegis ~  ⚡ϟ⚡ϟ⚡⛈ ✺❂❤❣</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Zeus, Hera, Apollo, Titans. All lovers of Ancient Greece.</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to ☘Misericordia☘ ~ The Serendipity Aegis ~  by: </span>
      Ancient Greece, classical upbringing&amp;edu, Athena (as a Goddess of Wisdom)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6603759-misericordia-the-serendipity-aegis?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2354528333" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3100090001639089165">8 April 2018<br/>Oh, gosh! I want it now! Can't wait for the publication! I expect it to be 5 stars... So, forgive me for prerating it already. It's like earmarking a favourite page...<br/>Remember, I'm a sucker for all things Ancient Greece. Classical mythos are my blood, my marrow... I grew up on them. Circe was never my fav, I felt she was a villain, a malicious sorceress for her part in Odyssey... Let's see if this book helps me get a different perception on this legendary gal.<br/><br/>15 April 2018<br/>So... This </span>
  <span id="freeText3100090001639089165" style="display:none">8 April 2018<br />Oh, gosh! I want it now! Can't wait for the publication! I expect it to be 5 stars... So, forgive me for prerating it already. It's like earmarking a favourite page...<br />Remember, I'm a sucker for all things Ancient Greece. Classical mythos are my blood, my marrow... I grew up on them. Circe was never my fav, I felt she was a villain, a malicious sorceress for her part in Odyssey... Let's see if this book helps me get a different perception on this legendary gal.<br /><br />15 April 2018<br />So... This is very different from how I imagined this. <br />First, we are acquainted with parental neglect. A one masquerading as a lot of different things.<br />Then we go straight to other things. Pharmakis, witch... Funny, how I never gave a thought to how Circe got to be at that island of hers. I guess I just thought she liked it a lot there. <br /><br />The ending… it was powerful, in its own right, in all the dimensions where it is unique. In our human world of today's insecure overachievers it seems weak and for the contemporaries, striving to do an inch better, to go a step further and to be a second faster - it might even seem defeatist. It's not so. It's the ultimate statement of freedom of self. One just needs to keep mind open to the realm of possibilities that Circe worked with, lived with and chose to do without.<br /><br />Q:<br />WHEN I WAS BORN, the name for what I was did not exist. (c)<br />Q:<br />That word, nymph, paced out the length and breadth of our futures. In our language, it means not just goddess, but bride. (c)<br />Q:<br />My father glowed bright as just-forged bronze, while Oceanos had been born with rheumy eyes and a white beard to his lap. Yet they were both Titans, and preferred each other’s company to those new-squeaking gods upon Olympus who had not seen the making of the world. (c)<br />Q:<br />Conditions, constrainment. These were novelties to my father, and gods love nothing more than novelty. (c)<br />Q:<br />“Come,” she said. “Let us make a better one.” (c)<br />Q:<br />My father had already departed for his chariot in the sky, and she was winding her hair with flowers, preparing to leave through the secret ways of water, to join her sisters on their grassy riverbanks. (c)<br />Q:<br />My father has never been able to imagine the world without himself in it. (c)<br />Q:<br />Divine days fall like water from a cataract, and I had not learned yet the mortal trick of counting them. (c)<br />Q:<br />hand. “You,” he said to my luminous sister Pasiphaë. “You will marry an eternal son of Zeus.” He used his prophecy voice, the one that spoke of future certainties. My mother glowed to hear it, thinking of the robes she would wear to Zeus’ feasts. (c)<br />Q:<br />Lampetia and Phaethousa, their names were. Radiant and Shining. (c)<br />Q:<br />Helios the Sun was bound to no will but his own, and none might say what he would do. <br />“Father,” I said that day, “are we late enough to kill astronomers?” (c)<br />Q:<br />It was my first lesson. Beneath the smooth, familiar face of things is another that waits to tear the world in two. (c) <br />Q: <br />Hours passed, perhaps days. But even gods cannot watch a whipping for eternity. (c)<br />Q:<br />Bold action and bold manner are not the same. (c)<br />Q:<br />How does your divinity feel? (c)<br />Q:<br />I do think he loved me a little. For before I could say the thousand humiliating things in my heart, all the proofs of passion I had hoarded, the crawling devotions I would do, I felt his power come around me. And with that same flick he had used upon the cushions, he sent me back to my rooms. (c)<br />Q:<br />Those flowers had made him his true being, which was blue, and finned, and not mine. (c)<br />Q:<br />I had made so many mistakes that I could not find my way back through their tangle to the first one. ... I felt a sickening unease that it went back further still, back to the first breath I ever drew. (c)<br />Q:<br />I can veil the sky itself. Just try to watch me. (c)<br />Q:<br />The torches burned like stars, and overhead the ceilings stretched high as the sky’s vault. For the last time, I watched all the gods and nymphs take their places. I felt dazed. (c)<br />Q:<br />My heart had leapt to see him as it always did. But he was like that column of water he had told me of once, cold and straight, sufficient to himself. (c)<br />Q:<br />The lands streamed by and the wind ran across my skin. I imagined pitching over that golden rail into the open air below. It would feel good, I thought, before I hit. (c)<br />Q:<br />All those years I had spent with them were like a stone tossed in a pool. Already, the ripples were gone. (c)<br />Q:<br />To be utterly alone. What worse punishment could there be, my family thought, than to be deprived of their divine presence? (с)<br />Q:<br />We were nymphs, not worth the trouble. (c)<br />Q:<br />Even our exiles live better than kings. You see how deep our strength runs? If you strike us, Olympian, we rise higher than before. (c)<br />Q:<br />Pharmakis, Aeëtes named me, witch, but all my strength was in those flowers, oceans away. (c)<br />Q:<br />No wonder I have been so slow, I thought. All this while, I have been a weaver without wool, a ship without the sea. Yet now look where I sail. (c)<br />Q:<br />The flowers, when they saw me, seemed to press forward like eager puppies, leaping and clamoring for my touch. I felt almost shy of them, but day by day I grew bolder, and at last I knelt in the damp earth before a clump of hellebore. (c)<br />Q:<br />I had a little pride, as I have said, and that was good. More would have been fatal. (c)<br />Q:<br />Let me say what sorcery is not: it is not divine power, which comes with a thought and a blink. It must be made and worked, planned and searched out, dug up, dried, chopped and ground, cooked, spoken over, and sung. Even after all that, it can fail, as gods do not. If my herbs are not fresh enough, if my attention falters, if my will is weak, the draughts go stale and rancid in my hands.<br />By rights, I should never have come to witchcraft. Gods hate all toil, it is their nature. ...<br />Witchcraft is nothing but such drudgery. Each herb must be found in its den, harvested at its time, grubbed up from the dirt, culled and stripped, washed and prepared. It must be handled this way, then that, to find out where its power lies. Day upon patient day, you must throw out your errors and begin again. So why did I not mind? Why did none of us mind? (c)<br />Q:<br />For a hundred generations, I had walked the world drowsy and dull, idle and at my ease. I left no prints, I did no deeds. Even those who had loved me a little did not care to stay. (c)<br />Q:<br />Then I learned that I could bend the world to my will, as a bow is bent for an arrow. I would have done that toil a thousand times to keep such power in my hands. (c)<br />Q:<br />One rose would give up its secrets if it were ground, another must be pressed, a third steeped. Each spell was a mountain to be climbed anew. All I could carry with me from last time was the knowledge that it could be done. (c)<br />Q:<br />I did not call dragons, or summon serpents. My earliest charms were silly things, whatever came into my head. (c)<br />Q:<br />My powers lapped upon themselves like waves. (c)<br />Q:<br />Into the air I said, Wherever you are, I hope you are finding your satisfaction.<br />Which, of course, now I know she was. (c)<br />Q:<br />My gaze seemed brighter, my face sharper, and there behind me paced my wild lion familiar. (c)<br />Q:<br />Well? What do you have to say to me? You threw me to the crows, but it turns out I prefer them to you. (c)<br />Q:<br />I felt myself tremble, but I would not let him see it. Great gods smell fear like sharks smell blood, and they will devour you for it just the same. (c)<br />Q:<br />But there was something in me that was sick of fear and awe, of gazing at the heavens and wondering what someone would allow me. (c)<br />Q:<br />Hermes’ mind was a thousand times sharper and more swift. It shone like light upon the waves, dazzling to blindness. (c)<br />Q:<br />Watching Zeus and Helios negotiate is always good entertainment. Like two volcanoes trying to decide if they should blow. (c)<br />Q:<br />He had fought in the great war, I remembered. He had seen the sky burn, and slain a giant whose head brushed the clouds. For all his lightness, I found I could imagine it. (c)<br />Q:<br />He made you want to spill your secrets. (c)<br />Q:<br />In a minute he had unraveled one of the great mysteries of my life. ... It was a shock, and yet there was part of me that felt something almost like recognition. (c)<br />Q:<br />“Do you know the name of this island?” I said.<br />“I would be a poor god of travelers if I did not know all the places in the world.” (c)<br />Q:<br />His eyes shone like dark gems held to light. They were black, one of the marks of deep-running power, from the line of the oldest gods. ... The same blood runs in all our veins. (c)<br />Q:<br />I owed him nothing. He would have of me only what I wanted to give. (c)<br />Q:<br />He was a poison snake, and I was another, and on such terms we pleased ourselves. (c)<br />Q:<br />My mother had gotten over her tears and added Mother of Witches to her titles, swanning with it among my aunts. (c)<br />Q:<br />We laughed over all of it, and when he left, I knew he told stories of me in turn: my dirt-black fingernails, my musky lion, the pigs that had begun coming to my door, truffling for slops and a scratch on the back. And, of course, how I had thrown myself upon him as a blushing virgin. (c)<br />Q:<br />“I suppose you might go and visit Prometheus,” I said. “You and your wings. Bring him something for comfort.”<br />“And why should I do that?”<br />“For novelty’s sake, of course. The first good deed in your dissolute life. Aren’t you curious what it would feel like?” (c)<br />Q:<br />My witchcraft, the island, my lion, all of them sprang from her transformation. There was no honesty in regretting what had given me my life. (c)<br />Q:<br />Monsters are a boon to gods. Imagine all the prayers. (c)<br />Q:<br />Would I be skimmed milk for crying, or a harpy with a heart of stone? There was nothing between. Anything else did not fit cleanly in the laughing tale he wanted to spin of it. (c)<br />Q:<br />I did not bring a torch. My eyes shone in the dark better than any owl’s. (c)<br />Q:<br />Oh, Father, did you know the gift you gave me? For that flower, so delicate it could dissolve beneath your stepping foot, carried within it the unyielding power of apotrope, the turning aside of evil. Curse-breaker. Ward and bulwark against ruin, worshipped like a god, for it was pure. The only thing in all the world you could be certain would not turn against you. (c)<br />Q:<br />Yet always I remembered.<br />Cold smoke, marked with my name. (c)<br />Q:<br />Of all the mortals on the earth, there are only a few the gods will ever hear of. Consider the practicalities. By the time we learn their names, they are dead. They must be meteors indeed to catch our attention. The merely good: you are dust to us. (c)<br />Q:<br />“What a relief to hear of my reprieve,” I said. “I cannot wait to be freed from my terrible prison.” The terraced hills around us glowed with spring. (c)<br />Q:<br />How could such variation endure, such endless iteration of minds and faces? Did the earth not go mad? (c)<br />Q:<br />That was what he desired most of all: to drive others into doubt, keep them wondering and fretting, stumbling behind his dancing feet. (c)<br />Q:<br />Fear of failure was the worst thing for any spell. (c)<br />Q:<br />But there was no wound she could give me that I had not already given myself. (c)<br />Q:<br />But now I understood more: he, too, knew what it was to make monsters. (c)<br />Q:<br />I did not walk as a mortal walks, but as a god, and the miles fell away beneath my feet. (c)<br />Q:<br />Most gods and mortals have lives that are tied to nothing; they tangle and wend now here, now there, according to no set plan. But then there are those who wear their destinies like nooses, whose lives run straight as planks, however they try to twist. It is these that our prophets may see. (c)<br />Q:<br />I felt an idea rise in me. I would need all the secret herbs of Dicte, and with them the strongest binding weeds, ilex root and withy, fennel and hemlock, aconite, hellebore. I would need as well the rest of my moly stores. I slipped through those trees unerring, hunting down each ingredient in its turn. If Artemis walked that night, she kept out of my way. (c)<br />Q:<br />I watched the knowledge settle on him. All the days ahead that he must be on his guard. He drew a breath. (c)<br />Q:<br />Griffins preened, delicate and regal, on the walls. The windows spilled sun. (c)<br />Q:<br />This was how mortals found fame, I thought. Through practice and diligence, tending their skills like gardens until they glowed beneath the sun. But gods are born of ichor and nectar, their excellences already bursting from their fingertips. So they find their fame by proving what they can mar: destroying cities, starting wars, breeding plagues and monsters. (c)<br /> Q:<br />Every step was perfect, like a gift she gave herself, and she smiled, receiving it. I wanted to seize her by the shoulders. Whatever you do, I wanted to say, do not be too happy. It will bring down fire on your head.<br />I said nothing, and let her dance. (c)<br />Q:<br />They are not naturally fools, it is only that they are caught between two scorpions. (c)<br />Q:<br />He becomes the great king who begets monsters and names them after himself. (c)<br />Q:<br />In thanks, it would know no comrade, no lover. It would never see the sun, never take a free step. There was nothing it might ever have in the world but hatred and darkness and its teeth. (c)<br />Q:<br />I had never thought of having children, but looking at him, for a moment I could imagine it. As if I peered into a well and far below glimpsed a flash of water. (c)<br />Q:<br />I turned away from my cell. I walked as a god, unseen, past the drowsing guards, past the night servants. (c)<br />Q:<br />“It was a gift, of course. Who else would have enjoyed seeing me bleed so much?”<br />“I can think of a thousand.” (c)<br />Q:<br />“I’ve been thinking,” she said, “of naming my son Asterion. Do you like it?”Starry one, it meant. “The prettiest name for a cannibal I ever heard.” (c)<br />Q:<br />My mind was bare, my skin bristling as if it would rise off my flesh (c)<br />Q:<br />Timidity creates nothing. (c)<br />Q:<br />I was a goddess, and he a mortal, and both of us were imprisoned. But I pressed his face into my mind, as seals are pressed in wax, so I could carry it with me. (c)<br />Q:<br />But in a solitary life, there are rare moments when another soul dips near yours, as stars once a year brush the earth. Such a constellation was he to me. (c)<br />Q:<br />The lion glared from my threshold. (c)<br />Q:<br />After my father’s halls, the island seemed to me the wildest, most giddy freedom. Its shores, its peaks, all of them yawned out to the horizon, filled up with magic. (c)<br />Q:<br />I had walked the earth for a hundred generations, yet I was still a child to myself. Rage and grief, thwarted desire, lust, self-pity: these are emotions gods know well. But guilt and shame, remorse, ambivalence, those are foreign countries to our kind, which must be learned stone by stone. (c)<br />Q:<br />After I had sailed away, he said, Minos and Pasiphaë’s eldest, Androgeos, had visited the mainland and been killed near Athens. By then, the people of Crete were restive at having to lose their sons and daughters every harvest, and were threatening revolt. Minos seized his opportunity. He demanded, as payment for his son, that the Athenian king send seven youths and seven maids to feed the monster, or else Crete’s mighty navy would bring war. (c)<br />Q:<br />No matter how vivid they were in life, no matter how brilliant, no matter the wonders they made, they came to dust and smoke. (c)<br />Q:<br />It was not magic they asked for, but the oldest rite of our kind. Katharsis. The cleansing by smoke and prayer, water and blood. (c)<br />Q:<br />I had no altar, but I did not need one: anywhere I was became my temple. (c)<br />Q:<br />I had expected her to be beautiful, for she walked like a queen of the gods, but it was an odd beauty, not like my mother’s or sister’s. Each of her features alone was nothing, her nose too sharp, her chin over-strong. Yet together they made a whole like the heart of a flame. You could not look away. (c)<br />Q:<br />Coming to him at night, in secret, that face of hers the only light. (c)<br />Q:<br />Now that I knew who she was, such meekness looked absurd on her, like a great eagle trying to hunch down to fit inside a sparrow’s nest. (c)<br />Q:<br />In his mind, he was already telling the tale to his court, to wide-eyed nobles and fainting maidens. He did not thank Medea for her aid; he scarcely looked at her. As if a demigoddess saving him at every turn was only his due. (c)<br />Q:<br />Perhaps that is why suppliants may not be questioned. How many of us would be granted pardon if our true hearts were known? (c)<br />Q:<br />I have wondered if they are merely empty shells, or if they understand what is being done to them and scream inside. (c)<br />Q:<br />“Does Jason know all this?”<br />“Of course he does not, are you mad? Every time he looked at me, he would think of poisons and burning skin. A man wants a wife like new grass, fresh and green.” (c)<br />Q:<br />Her skin was cool, as if she had been walking a long time in the wind. (c)<br />Q:<br />She stood fixed in her outrage, bright and defiant. ... Just so must I have seemed to my grandmother when she said to me: Those are two different things. (c)<br />Q:<br />They drew up the anchor and sailed into the darkness, their path lit only by the veiled moon and the unwavering gold of Medea’s eyes. (c)<br />Q:<br />Use your imagination, they must be good for something. Take them to your bed.”<br />“That is absurd,” I said. “They would run screaming.”<br />“Nymphs always do,” he said. “But I’ll tell you a secret: they are terrible at getting away.” (с)<br />Q:<br />The truth is, men make terrible pigs. (c)<br />Q:<br />Humbling women seems to me a chief pastime of poets. As if there can be no story unless we crawl and weep. (c)<br />Q:<br />When he talked, he was lawyer and bard and crossroads charlatan at once, arguing his case, entertaining, pulling back the veil to show you the secrets of the world. (c)<br />Q:<br />A door that did not open at his knock was a novelty in its own right, and a kind of relief as well. All the world confessed to him. He confessed to me. (c)<br />Q:<br />I led him to my room and spoke there the knowledge that had been rising in me all day, quick and unceasing, like bubbles from a stream. (c)<br />Q:<br />It was one of my favorite things about him: how he always fought for his chance. (c)<br />Q:<br />Such were my years then. I would like to say that all the while I waited to break out, but the truth is, I’m afraid I might have floated on, believing those dull miseries were all there was, until the end of days. (c)<br />Q:<br />Odysseus, son of Laertes, the great traveler, prince of wiles and tricks and a thousand ways. He showed me his scars, and in return he let me pretend that I had none. (c)<br />Q:<br />I would show him all the wonders I had gathered for him, this island and its sky, the fruits and sheep, the waves and lions. The perfect solitude that would never be loneliness again. (c)<br />Q:<br />I laid him in his crib, then drew it close to the fire and set my lions and wolves around it. They could not stop a god, but most divinities are cowards. Claws and teeth might buy me a little time. (с)<br />Q:<br />Do not listen to your enemy, Odysseus had once told me. Look at them. It will tell you everything. <br />I looked. Armed and armored, she was, from head to foot, helmet, spear, aigis, greaves. A terrifying vision: the goddess of war, ready for battle. But why had she assembled such a panoply against me, who knew nothing of combat? Unless there was something else she feared, something that made her feel somehow stripped and weak. <br />Instinct carried me forward, the thousand hours I had spent in my father’s halls, and with Odysseus polymetis, man of so many wiles. (c)<br />Q:<br />But Athena had no babe, and she never would. Her only love was reason. And that has never been the same as wisdom. (c)<br />Q:<br />“We are not our blood,” ... “A witch once told me that.” (c)<br />Q:<br />I did not pretend to be a mortal. I showed my lambent, yellow eyes at every turn. None of it made a difference. I was alone and a woman, that was all that mattered. (c)</span>
  <a data-text-id="3100090001639089165" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781408890080" href="/book/show/32993458-circe">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2306223297" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15495004724249381619">Amazing book, utterly fascinating and compelling. Quite unusual and exceptional story. I like Circe, she is a strong woman (sorry, goddess, or witch goddess) and a courageous fighter. I like her style. The book cover says "an intoxicating tale of gods and heroes, magic and monsters, survival and transformation". Yes, that about sums it up, this great story. The only remark I would have is that the middle part, when Circe is with Odysseus, is a bit of a small dip, which is why this book just miss</span>
  <span id="freeText15495004724249381619" style="display:none">Amazing book, utterly fascinating and compelling. Quite unusual and exceptional story. I like Circe, she is a strong woman (sorry, goddess, or witch goddess) and a courageous fighter. I like her style. The book cover says "an intoxicating tale of gods and heroes, magic and monsters, survival and transformation". Yes, that about sums it up, this great story. The only remark I would have is that the middle part, when Circe is with Odysseus, is a bit of a small dip, which is why this book just misses the 5 star mark for me.<br />But other than that, a great book to read, almost breathless reading. Challenging and worthwhile! Four plus for me &amp; recommended.<br /><i>When I was born, the name for what I was did not exist. They called me nymph, assuming I would be like my mother and aunts and thousand cousins. Least of the lesser goddesses, our powers were so modest they could scarcely ensure our eternities....</i></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2071219150" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2071219150" />
    <a title="Charlotte May" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14002983-charlotte-may"><img alt="Charlotte May" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596906925p2/14002983.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2071219150?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charlotte May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charlotte May" href="/user/show/14002983-charlotte-may">Charlotte May</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781408890080" href="/book/show/32993458-circe">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14002983-charlotte-may?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14002983-charlotte-may?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14002983-charlotte-may?shelf=retelling-fairytale">retelling-fairytale</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14002983-charlotte-may?shelf=romance">romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2071219150" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10600094832784929662"><i> "But in a solitary life, there are rare moments when another soul dips near yours, as stars once a year brush the earth." </i><br/><br/>Well this was just jam packed with loads of mythology, gods and goddesses and characters from antiquity!<br/>I loved spotting so many memorable names and stories, woven expertly into Circe's tale.<br/><br/>Daedalus and Icarus<br/>Prometheus and his poor poor liver<br/>Circe's father - the Sun god Helios and his perfect white cattle<br/>Her sister Pasiphae, mother of Aridane, who befriends and aids Theseu</span>
  <span id="freeText10600094832784929662" style="display:none"><i> "But in a solitary life, there are rare moments when another soul dips near yours, as stars once a year brush the earth." </i><br /><br />Well this was just jam packed with loads of mythology, gods and goddesses and characters from antiquity!<br />I loved spotting so many memorable names and stories, woven expertly into Circe's tale.<br /><br />Daedalus and Icarus<br />Prometheus and his poor poor liver<br />Circe's father - the Sun god Helios and his perfect white cattle<br />Her sister Pasiphae, mother of Aridane, who befriends and aids Theseus in his destruction of the minotaur.<br />Her brother Aaetes, father of Medea - lover of Jason, and history's original crazy lady. <br />And of course the wanderer Odysseus, who along with his men discover Circe's island.<br /><br />Circe is a very well known character in mythology. The witch who turns men into pigs - she is portrayed as a villain, in only the brief chapter she has within <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1384.The_Odyssey" title="The Odyssey by Homer" rel="nofollow noopener">The Odyssey</a>. Madeline Miller gives new life to the character of Circe. We hear about her upbringing as a daughter of a Titan, her awful relatives and the absolute disassociation of the Olympian gods.<br />When she is discovered to have certain 'witch like' powers, her father Helios banishes her to an island, to live out her days forever.<br /><br /><i> "You threw me to the crows, but it turns out I prefer them to you." </i><br /><br />She receives many visitors including Hermes, watches the coming and going of so many mortals. It seems divinity can be really damn exhausting. Without giving away all the different story aspects, she is included in so many lives, not least that of Odysseus; who is certainly shown in a different light in this book.<br /><br />Overall a fascinating tale filled with myths and characters. If you are familiar with Circe or not, it is an exciting read, and she is a truly great protagonist to meet.<br /><br /><i> "Those who fight against prophecy only draw it more tightly around their throats." </i><br /><br />4 stars!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10600094832784929662" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_54331802'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/54331802-dueling-fates"><img alt="Dueling Fates" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1593489647l/54331802._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mAwhzFWmGLRBfFjJwQlRzckSb4W187RaW4UaivBtHV3Dt1NjZTe7wPF/j1yuQXDOaoYi/Q0zIipmBOjmTAqRqg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('OWexhr8TES5/QZbcXRGsLCuChuqDN93AaqYf02RlVN1i3MMpj4KyWs9CQUkyWY0viBbLkjv3S7BXJ+2/2ALYKg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

She was queen of one of Troy\'s neighboring kingdoms, until Achilles, Greece\'s greatest warrior, sacked her city and murdered her husband and brothers. Briseis becomes Achilles\'s concubine, a prize of battle, and must adjust quickly in order to survive a radically different life, as one of the many conquered women who serve the Greek army. \n\nWhen Agamemnon, the brutal political leader of the Greek forces, demands Briseis for himself, she finds herself caught between the two most powerful of the Greeks. Achilles refuses to fight in protest, and the Greeks begin to lose ground to their Trojan opponents. Keenly observant and coolly unflinching about the daily horrors of war, Briseis finds herself in an unprecedented position, able to observe the two men driving the Greek army in what will become their final confrontation, deciding the fate not only of Briseis\'s people but also of the ancient world at large.\n\nBriseis is just one among thousands of women living behind the scenes in this war—the slaves and prostitutes, the nurses, the women who lay out the dead—all of them erased by history. With breathtaking historical detail and luminous prose, Pat Barker brings the teeming world of the Greek camp to vivid life. She offers nuanced, complex portraits of characters and stories familiar from mythology, which, seen from Briseis\'s perspective, are rife with newfound revelations. Barker\'s latest builds on her decades-long study of war and its impact on individual lives—and it is nothing short of magnificent.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"6946998754055243754\" href=\"#\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/176372.Madeline_Miller">About Madeline Miller</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/176372.Madeline_Miller"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1322861610p3/176372.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/176372.Madeline_Miller">Madeline Miller
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
21,716
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16896860784275812699">Madeline Miller was born in Boston and grew up in New York City and Philadelphia. She attended Brown University, where she earned her BA and MA in Classics. For the last ten years she has been teaching and tutoring Latin, Greek and Shakespeare to high school students. She has also studied at the University of Chicago’s Committee on Social Thought, and in the Dramaturgy department at Yale School of</span>
  <span id="freeText16896860784275812699" style="display:none">Madeline Miller was born in Boston and grew up in New York City and Philadelphia. She attended Brown University, where she earned her BA and MA in Classics. For the last ten years she has been teaching and tutoring Latin, Greek and Shakespeare to high school students. She has also studied at the University of Chicago’s Committee on Social Thought, and in the Dramaturgy department at Yale School of Drama, where she focused on the adaptation of classical texts to modern forms. She currently lives in Cambridge, MA, where she teaches and writes. <i>The Song of Achilles</i> is her first novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16896860784275812699" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
